Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Defining Kitab and Taharah
Defining 'kitab' and 'taharah' and listing components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and technical definitions of 'kitab' and 'taharah' and identify the structural components (cause, pillar, ruling, instruments, conditions) of taharah in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Taharah is both literal cleanliness and legal purification; its definition sets the foundation for all acts of worship.

This opening passage of Kitab al-Taharah defines the very terms that govern the entire section. Understanding 'kitab' as a collection of issues and 'taharah' as purification from both physical and legal impurity is essential. The author also outlines the key components: cause, pillar, ruling, instruments, and conditions.

Guided Path
1
Define 'kitab'
Linguistically, kitab means 'collection' (from gathering). Technically, it is a collection of independent legal issues (specific words indicating collected issues). This is the chosen definition per al-Hamawi.
Note the shift from linguistic to technical meaning.
2
Define 'taharah' linguistically
With fatha (al-taharah) it means cleanliness; with kasra (al-tahirah) it means the instrument; with damma (al-tuharah) it means the leftover purification water.
The different vowel readings indicate related meanings.
3
Define 'taharah' technically
Technically, taharah is cleanliness of the place (body, clothing, or location) from either real impurity (khabath, e.g., urine) or legal impurity (hadath, e.g., lack of wudu).
This covers both physical najasa and ritual impurity.
4
Identify the components of taharah
The text mentions: (1) cause of obligation (ikhtilaf: hadath/khabath, or intending prayer, or obligation itself); (2) pillar (rukn): washing the limbs or place; (3) ruling (hukm): making permissible what was otherwise prohibited; (4) instruments: water, earth (tayammum), and annexes; (5) conditions: divided into conditions of obligation and conditions of validity.
These components are standard in Hanafi discussions of purification.
5
Note the singular usage
The author uses the singular 'taharah' despite many types (clothing, place, body) because it is a verbal noun and a genus covering all types.
This is a concise stylistic choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة : هو مركب إضافي، خبر لمبتدأ محذوف تقديره هذا كتاب الطهارة"، ولك نصبه على أنه مفعول لفعل محذوف، تقديره: "هاك أو خذ كتاب الطهارة. وحده لقباً موقوف على معرفة مفرد به، فالكتاب: مصدر ككتابة وكتبة ومعناه لغة: الجمع وهو ضم شيء إلى شيء، ومنه الكتيبة للجيش المجتمع، وكتبت الخيل، أي جمعت، وعرفاً: جمع مسائل مستقلة، أي ألفاظ مخصوصة دالة على مسائل مجموعة، وهذا هو المختار كما قال الحموي. وأما "الطهارة": فهي بالفتح النظافة، وبالكسر الآلة، وبالضم فضل ما يتطهر به، واصطلاحاً: نظافة المحل عن النجاسة الحقيقية أو الحكمية واختلف في سبب :وجوبها فقيل الحدث والخبث، وقيل: إقامة الصلاة وإرادتها، وقيل: وجوبها، وركنها غسل الأعضاء أو المحل، وحكمها استباحة ما لا يحل إلا بها، وآلتها: الماء والتراب والملحق بهما، وشرائطها تنقسم إلى شروط وجوب وشروط صحة كما بين في المطولات، ثم إن المصنف اكتفى بلفظ الواحد مع كثرة أنواع الطهارة مثل طهارة الثوب وطهارة المكان وطهارة البدن وغيرها؛ لكونها مصدراً، وأصله أن لا يثنى ولا يجمع؛ لأنه جنس يشمل جميع الأنواع والأفراد مع كونه أخصر في العبارة، في تقديم كتاب الطهارة على سائر الكتب أن الصلاة عماد الدين وأعظم أركان الإسلام بعد الإيمان، فكانت أحق بالتقديم، والطهارة شرطها، والشرط مقدم على المشروط، ثم اختصت الطهارة بالبداءة من بين سائر الشروط؛ لأنها أهم من غيرها؛ ولأنها لا تسقط بعد زمن الأعذار غالباً، ثم قدم بيان الوضوء الذي هو طهارة صغرى على الغسل الذي هو طهارة كبرى، إما اقتداء بالكتاب العزيز، وإما باعتبار شدة الاحتياج. (فتح)
'Kitab al-Taharah': It is an idafa compound, a predicate of an omitted subject whose implied form is 'This is Kitab al-Taharah', or it is in the accusative as an object of an omitted verb, whose implied form is 'Take Kitab al-Taharah'. Its definition as a term depends on understanding its individual parts. 'Kitab' (book) is a verbal noun like kitaba and kitba; its linguistic meaning is 'to collect' which is joining one thing to another. From this is 'kateeba' for a gathered army, and 'katabt al-khayl' meaning I collected (the horses). Its technical meaning: a collection of independent issues, i.e., specific words indicating collected issues, and this is the chosen definition as al-Hamawi said. 'Al-Taharah': with fatha it means cleanliness, with kasra the instrument, with damma the leftover water used for purification. Technically: cleanliness of the place from real or legal impurity. There is disagreement over its cause of obligation: some say hadath and khabath, others say the establishment of prayer and intending it, others say its obligation. Its pillar is washing the limbs or the place. Its ruling is making permissible what is not permissible except by it. Its instruments are water, earth, and what is annexed to them. Its conditions divide into conditions of obligation and conditions of validity as explained in the expanded works. Then the author sufficed with the singular form despite the many types of taharah, such as purification of clothing, place, body, etc., because it is a verbal noun and its origin is not to be dualized or pluralized; it is a genus that includes all types and individuals, while also being more concise. Regarding the placement of Kitab al-Taharah before all other books: because prayer is the pillar of religion and the greatest of the pillars of Islam after faith, so it is most deserving of precedence, and taharah is its condition, and the condition precedes the conditioned. Then taharah was specified for beginning among all conditions because it is more important than others, and because it generally does not fall away after the time of excuses. Then the explanation of wudu (which is a minor purification) is placed before ghusl (which is a major purification), either following the Noble Book or considering the intensity of need. (Fath)
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'kitab' and 'taharah' and explains why the book on purity is placed first. Linguistically, 'kitab' means collection; technically, it is a compilation of independent legal issues. 'Taharah' linguistically means cleanliness; technically, it is cleanliness from both real physical impurity (najasa) and legal impurity (hadath). The author mentions the different opinions on what necessitates taharah: some say it is the state of impurity (hadath and khabath), others say it is the act of prayer itself. The pillar (rukn) of taharah is washing the relevant body parts or area. The ruling (hukm) of taharah is that it permits acts otherwise forbidden without it. The instruments are water, earth (for tayammum), and things similar. The conditions are divided into conditions of obligation and conditions of validity. The author notes that 'taharah' is used in the singular to encompass all types. The reason for placing this book first is that prayer is the greatest pillar after faith, and taharah is its condition; a condition is logically prior. Moreover, taharah is the most important condition as it is generally never excused even in times of hardship. Within the book, wudu (minor ablution) precedes ghusl (major ablution), either following the Qur'anic order or because the need for wudu is more frequent.

Key Terms
الطهارة (al-taharah): Linguistically: cleanliness; technically: cleanliness from real or legal impurity.
الحدث (al-hadath): A legal state of impurity necessitating purification.
الخبث (al-khabath): Actual physical impurity (najasa) on the body, clothing, or place.
الكتاب (al-kitab): Linguistically: collection; technically: a collection of independent legal issues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the technical definition of 'taharah' according to the text?
A) Cleanliness of the heart from sin
B) Cleanliness of the place from real or legal impurity
C) Performing wudu with water
D) Removing najasa from clothing

Key Takeaway
Taharah is cleanliness from both physical impurity (khabath) and legal impurity (hadath), and its study begins the fiqh text because it is the condition for prayer.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Why Kitab al-Taharah Comes First
Justifying the placement of Kitab al-Taharah first and the order within it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rationale for placing the book on purification before all other books and justify the order of wudu before ghusl.

Core Idea
The condition (taharah) is logically and pedagogically prior to the conditioned (prayer) — thus the book of purity opens the fiqh text.

After defining taharah, the author explains why this book is placed before all others. The reasoning is rooted in the paramount importance of prayer and the fact that taharah is its prerequisite. Moreover, within taharah, minor purification (wudu) precedes major purification (ghusl) due to more frequent need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main reason
Prayer (salah) is the pillar of religion and the greatest of the pillars of Islam after faith. Therefore, it deserves precedence in the fiqh text.
This reasoning establishes prayer's centrality.
2
Understand the condition-conditioned logic
Taharah is a condition (shart) for prayer. In legal ordering, the condition is logically prior to the conditioned (mashrut). So taharah is placed before prayer (and hence before all books since prayer is the most important act).
This is a standard principle in fiqh arrangement.
3
Recognize why taharah is chosen over other conditions
Taharah is the most important condition for prayer because it generally does not fall away even in times of excuses (except in extreme cases like tayammum). It is also a constant requirement.
Other conditions like facing the qibla can be excused in some situations, but taharah is almost always required.
4
Explain the order within the book
Within Kitab al-Taharah, the author places wudu (minor purification) before ghusl (major purification). The text gives two possible reasons: (1) following the order of the Noble Quran (where Allah mentions wudu before ghusl in Surah al-Maidah), or (2) because the need for wudu is more frequent and intense (most acts require wudu, while ghusl is less frequent).
Both reasons are plausible and complementary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة : هو مركب إضافي، خبر لمبتدأ محذوف تقديره هذا كتاب الطهارة"، ولك نصبه على أنه مفعول لفعل محذوف، تقديره: "هاك أو خذ كتاب الطهارة. وحده لقباً موقوف على معرفة مفرد به، فالكتاب: مصدر ككتابة وكتبة ومعناه لغة: الجمع وهو ضم شيء إلى شيء، ومنه الكتيبة للجيش المجتمع، وكتبت الخيل، أي جمعت، وعرفاً: جمع مسائل مستقلة، أي ألفاظ مخصوصة دالة على مسائل مجموعة، وهذا هو المختار كما قال الحموي. وأما "الطهارة": فهي بالفتح النظافة، وبالكسر الآلة، وبالضم فضل ما يتطهر به، واصطلاحاً: نظافة المحل عن النجاسة الحقيقية أو الحكمية واختلف في سبب :وجوبها فقيل الحدث والخبث، وقيل: إقامة الصلاة وإرادتها، وقيل: وجوبها، وركنها غسل الأعضاء أو المحل، وحكمها استباحة ما لا يحل إلا بها، وآلتها: الماء والتراب والملحق بهما، وشرائطها تنقسم إلى شروط وجوب وشروط صحة كما بين في المطولات، ثم إن المصنف اكتفى بلفظ الواحد مع كثرة أنواع الطهارة مثل طهارة الثوب وطهارة المكان وطهارة البدن وغيرها؛ لكونها مصدراً، وأصله أن لا يثنى ولا يجمع؛ لأنه جنس يشمل جميع الأنواع والأفراد مع كونه أخصر في العبارة، في تقديم كتاب الطهارة على سائر الكتب أن الصلاة عماد الدين وأعظم أركان الإسلام بعد الإيمان، فكانت أحق بالتقديم، والطهارة شرطها، والشرط مقدم على المشروط، ثم اختصت الطهارة بالبداءة من بين سائر الشروط؛ لأنها أهم من غيرها؛ ولأنها لا تسقط بعد زمن الأعذار غالباً، ثم قدم بيان الوضوء الذي هو طهارة صغرى على الغسل الذي هو طهارة كبرى، إما اقتداء بالكتاب العزيز، وإما باعتبار شدة الاحتياج. (فتح)

Commentary
This passage defines the terms 'kitab' and 'taharah' and explains why the book on purity is placed first. Linguistically, 'kitab' means collection; technically, it is a compilation of independent legal issues. 'Taharah' linguistically means cleanliness; technically, it is cleanliness from both real physical impurity (najasa) and legal impurity (hadath). Key Terms
الشرط (al-shart): A condition that must be fulfilled for an act to be valid.
المشروط (al-mashrut): The conditioned act that depends on the condition.
طهارة صغرى (taharah sughra): Minor purification, i.e., wudu.
طهارة كبرى (taharah kubra): Major purification, i.e., ghusl.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is wudu placed before ghusl within Kitab al-Taharah?
A) Because wudu is easier than ghusl
B) Because wudu is mentioned before ghusl in the Quran or because the need for wudu is greater
C) Because wudu is always obligatory while ghusl is not
D) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) performed wudu more often

Key Takeaway
Kitab al-Taharah is placed first because taharah is the condition for prayer, the greatest pillar after faith, and the condition must precede the conditioned.

Definition and Obligation of Wudu in the Hanafi School
Defining 'fard' and 'wudu' and proving the obligation of wudu.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of 'fard' and 'wudu', explain the obligation of wudu using the Quranic proof, and address the objection regarding the timing of the revelation of the wudu verse.

Core Idea
Wudu is a fard established by a definitive proof; the Quranic command obligates it, and it applied even before the Madani verse through other revelation.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq lays the foundation for understanding wudu: what it is, why it is obligatory, and how the obligation was established. The author first defines the key terms 'fard' and 'wudu', then presents the Quranic evidence and addresses a critical objection about the timing of the verse's revelation. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of 'fard'
Identify the linguistic meaning (تقدير, determination) and the technical meaning (a binding ruling established by definitive proof). Note the distinction between the definitive fard and the practical fard, and the author's preferred encompassing definition.
The Hanafi school distinguishes fard (definitive proof) from wajib (probable proof).
2
Understand the definition of 'wudu'
Recognize the linguistic derivation from 'wada'ah' (cleanliness) and the technical definition as purification of the four limbs in a specific manner. Note the different vowellings and meanings.
3
Identify the proof of obligation
Locate the Quranic proof: فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ (al-Ma'idah 5:6). Apply the Hanafi usul principle that a command (al-amr) implies obligation (al-wujub) unless there is a contrary indicator. Since the verse is qat'i in transmission and meaning, the obligation is fard.
The imperative verb 'فاغسلوا' directly indicates obligation.
4
Analyze the objection and the response
Examine the hypothetical objection: the wudu verse is Madani, but prayer was obligated in Makkah. Resolve that wudu could have been established by non-Qur'anic revelation (wahy ghayr matlu) or by following the laws of previous prophets, as supported by the hadith that the Prophet said: 'This is my wudu and the wudu of the prophets before me.'
This defense shows that the obligation of wudu is continuous and not dependent on the time of the verse's revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فرض الوضوء أي فرض للوضوء، و"الفرض" في اللغة التقدير، وفي الشرع عبارة عن حكم مقدر لا يحتمل زيادة ولا نقصانا؛ لأنه ثبت بدليل قطعي لا شبهة فيه، وهذا تعريف للفرض القطعي دون العملي، والأولى أن يفسر الفرض بما لزم فعله؛ ليعم النوعين والوضوء" بضم الواو، مأخوذ من الوضاءة، وهي النظافة مطلقاً، وبفتح الواو اسم لما يحصل به النظافة، وبكسر الواو اسم للموضع الذي يتوضأ فيه، وفي الشرع: نظافة محل مخصوص وهو الأعضاء الأربعة على الوجه المخصوص الذي بينه الشارع في قوله: ﴿فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ (المائدة:1) فإن قيل: آية الوضوء مدنية بالاتفاق والصلاة فرضت بمكة، فيلزم كون الصلاة بلا وضوء إلى حين نزولها؟ قلنا: لا يلزم؛ لأنه يجوز أن يثبت الوضوء بالوحي الغير المتلو، والأخذ من الشرائع السابقة، كما حين توضأ ثلاثاً ثلاثاً، قال: "هذا وضوئي ووضوء الأنبياء من قبلي". (فتح) روي أنه عليه السلام توضأ ثلاثاً ثلاثاً، قال: "هذا وضوئي ووضوء الأنبياء من قبلي". (فتح) روي أنه عليه السلام
'Fard al-wudu' meaning the obligation of wudu. And 'al-fard' in language means 'determination/estimation', and in the Sharia it is an expression of a determined ruling that does not admit increase or decrease, because it is established by a definitive proof (dalil qati) free from any doubt. This definition is for the definitive fard, not the practical; the better view is to define fard as what must be done, to include both types. And 'al-wudu' with damm on the waw, derived from 'al-wada'ah' which means cleanliness absolutely; with fatha on the waw it is a name for the thing by which cleanliness is achieved; with kasra on the waw it is a name for the place where one performs wudu. In the Sharia: cleanliness of a specific place, namely the four limbs, in the specific manner that the Lawgiver has explained in His saying: 'Wash your faces' (al-Ma'idah:1). If it is said: The verse of wudu is unanimously Madani, and prayer was obligated in Makkah, so it would follow that prayer was without wudu until its revelation? We say: it does not follow, because it is permissible that wudu was established by non-recited revelation (wahy ghayr matlu) and by taking from previous laws, as when he (peace be upon him) performed wudu three times three times and said: 'This is my wudu and the wudu of the prophets before me.' (Fath) It is narrated that he (peace be upon him) performed wudu three times three times and said...
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion of the obligations of wudu. It defines 'fard' and 'wudu'. 'Fard' linguistically means 'determination'; technically, it is a binding ruling established by a definitive proof. The author notes that some define fard as what does not admit increase or decrease, but the better definition is simply what must be done, covering both definitive (qati) and practical (amali) obligations. 'Wudu' linguistically derives from 'wada'ah' (cleanliness); technically, it is the purification of specific body parts (the four limbs) in a specific manner. The primary proof is the Qur'anic command: 'Wash your faces...' (Surah al-Ma'idah, verse 6). However, an objection arises: this verse was revealed in Madinah, but prayer was obligated in Makkah; does this mean prayer was performed without wudu? The answer is no: wudu could have been established by non-Qur'anic revelation (wahy ghayr matlu) or by following the laws of previous prophets. This is supported by the hadith where the Prophet said, 'This is my wudu and the wudu of the prophets before me.'

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): Linguistically: determination/estimation. Technically: a binding ruling established by definitive proof.
الوضوء (al-wudu): Linguistically: cleanliness. Technically: purification of the four limbs in a specific manner.
الوحي الغير المتلو (al-wahy ghayr al-matlu): Non-recited revelation; revelation that is not part of the Qur'an, such as hadith qudsi or prophetic traditions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How was the obligation of wudu established before the revelation of the verse in Surah al-Ma'idah?
A) It was not established, so prayer was without wudu.
B) Through non-Qur'anic revelation and following previous prophets' laws.
C) Through qiyas (analogy) on other acts of worship.
D) Through ijma (consensus) of the Companions.

Key Takeaway
Wudu is a fard established by definitive textual proof (Quran) and supported by prophetic practice, ensuring its obligation was continuous from the beginning of Islam.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Introduction to Kitab al-Tahara: Definition and Precedence
Defining kitab and tahara, justifying precedence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'kitab' and 'tahara' in Hanafi terminology and explain why the Book of Tahara precedes other books in fiqh works.

Core Idea
Tahara is the condition for salah; the condition logically precedes the conditioned, hence its placement first.

This lesson unpacks the opening of a Hanafi fiqh text. The author begins with Kitab al-Tahara, defining both words and justifying why purification is addressed before any other topic. Understanding this structure helps you see the logical ordering of fiqh: conditions before acts. We will walk through the definition of 'kitab' as a technical term, the comprehensive meaning of 'tahara', and the reasoning behind its primacy.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the grammatical construction
Identify how 'Kitab al-Tahara' is a genitive compound (idafa). Understand that it can be read as a predicate of a deleted subject ('this is the book of tahara') or as an object of a deleted verb ('take the book of tahara'). This grammatical awareness is key to interpreting classical text titles.
This is typical of matn openings; it sets the tone for a precise legal work.
2
Define 'kitab' linguistically and technically
Linguistically, 'kitab' means collection or writing; technically, it is a collection of independent legal issues expressed in specific phrases. The passage notes that this is the chosen view (al-mukhtar) according to al-Hamawi.
The technical definition emphasizes that a kitab contains distinct but related masail.
3
Define 'tahara' linguistically and technically
Linguistically, tahara with fatha means cleanliness, with kasra the instrument, with damma leftover water. Technically, it is cleanliness from actual filth (najasa) and legal impurity (hadath). Recognize that the singular form covers all types (body, garment, place) because it is a verbal noun (masdar).
The verbal noun usage is concise and comprehensive; this is a Hanafi stylistic choice.
4
Identify the components of tahara mentioned
The passage lists: its cause (sabab) – disputed among scholars; its integral (rukn) – washing the limbs or place; its ruling (hukm) – making lawful what is otherwise forbidden; its instrument (ala) – water, earth, and annexes; and its conditions (shurut) – divided into conditions of obligation and conditions of validity. Note that the conditions are not detailed here; they are elaborated in longer works.
This structural breakdown is a hallmark of Hanafi systematic fiqh.
5
Explain the precedence of Kitab al-Tahara
The author argues that salah is the greatest pillar after iman, and tahara is its condition. The condition must precede the conditioned in presentation. Furthermore, tahara is singled out among other conditions because it is more important and not waived after the period of excuses. Finally, within tahara, wudu (lesser) precedes ghusl (greater) due to greater need and following Quranic order.
This logical ordering is a key feature of Hanafi text organization; it helps the student learn progressively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة : هو مركب إضافي، خبر لمبتدأ محذوف تقديره هذا كتاب الطهارة، ولك نصبه على أنه مفعول لفعل محذوف، تقديره: هاك أو خذ كتاب الطهارة. وحده لقباً موقوف على معرفة مفرد به، فالكتاب: مصدر ككتابة وكتبة ومعناه لغة: الجمع وهو ضم شيء إلى شيء، ومنه الكتيبة للجيش المجتمع، وكتبت الخيل، أي جمعت، وعرفاً: جمع مسائل مستقلة، أي ألفاظ مخصوصة دالة على مسائل مجموعة، وهذا هو المختار كما قال الحموي. وأما الطهارة: فهي بالفتح النظافة، وبالكسر الآلة، وبالضم فضل ما يتطهر به، واصطلاحاً: نظافة المحل عن النجاسة الحقيقية أو الحكمية واختلف في سبب وجوبها فقيل الحدث والخبث، وقيل: إقامة الصلاة وإرادتها، وقيل: وجوبها، وركنها غسل الأعضاء أو المحل، وحكمها استباحة ما لا يحل إلا بها، وآلتها: الماء والتراب والملحق بهما، وشرائطها تنقسم إلى شروط وجوب وشروط صحة كما بين في المطولات، ثم إن المصنف اكتفى بلفظ الواحد مع كثرة أنواع الطهارة مثل طهارة الثوب وطهارة المكان وطهارة البدن وغيرها؛ لكونها مصدراً، وأصله أن لا يثنى ولا يجمع؛ لأنه جنس يشمل جميع الأنواع والأفراد مع كونه أخصر في العبارة، في تقديم كتاب الطهارة على سائر الكتب أن الصلاة عماد الدين وأعظم أركان الإسلام بعد الإيمان، فكانت أحق بالتقديم، والطهارة شرطها، والشرط مقدم على المشروط، ثم اختصت الطهارة بالبداءة من بين سائر الشروط؛ لأنها أهم من غيرها؛ ولأنها لا تسقط بعد زمن الأعذار غالباً، ثم قدم بيان الوضوء الذي هو طهارة صغرى على الغسل الذي هو طهارة كبرى، إما اقتداء بالكتاب العزيز، وإما باعتبار شدة الاحتياج. (فتح)
Kitab al-Tahara: It is a genitive compound, predicate of a deleted subject whose meant is 'this is the book of tahara', and it is allowed to be accusative as an object of a deleted verb whose meant is 'here is' or 'take the book of tahara'. Its definition as a title is dependent on understanding its individual component. So 'kitab' is a verbal noun like 'kitaba' and 'kitba', and its linguistic meaning is collection, which is gathering one thing to another, from which comes 'kutiba' for the gathered army, and 'kutibat al-khayl' meaning they were gathered. Technically, it is a collection of independent issues, i.e., specific expressions indicating collected issues, and this is the chosen view as al-Hamawi said. As for 'tahara', with fatha it means cleanliness, with kasra the instrument, with damma the leftover water used for purification. Technically, it is the cleanliness of the place from actual or legal impurity. There is disagreement on the cause of its obligation: some say it is hadath and khabath, others say it is the establishment of prayer and intending it, others say it is its obligation. Its integral (rukn) is washing the limbs or the place. Its legal effect is making permissible what is not permissible except by it. Its instrument is water, earth, and what is annexed to them. Its conditions are divided into conditions of obligation and conditions of validity as explained in the detailed works. Then the author contented himself with the singular word despite the many types of tahara, such as tahara of the garment, tahara of the place, tahara of the body, etc., because it is a verbal noun and its original rule is that it is not dualized or pluralized because it is a genus encompassing all types and individuals along with being briefer in expression. Regarding presenting the Book of Tahara before other books: because prayer is the pillar of religion and the greatest pillar of Islam after faith, so it is most deserving of being presented, and tahara is its condition, and the condition precedes the conditioned. Then tahara was singled out for beginning from among all conditions because it is more important than others and because it is not waived after the period of excuses usually. Then he presented the explanation of wudu which is a lesser purification over ghusl which is a greater purification, either following the Noble Qur'an or considering the intensity of need. (Fath)
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a front-matter commentary explaining the title and structure of the book. It opens by analyzing the grammatical construction of 'Kitab al-Tahara', clarifying that 'kitab' here means a collected work of independent legal issues, and 'tahara' is defined linguistically and technically. The author then gives a comprehensive definition of tahara in Islamic law: cleanliness from both actual najasah (filth) and legal ritual impurity (hadath). He notes disagreements on whether the obligation of tahara stems from hadath itself, the intention to pray, or the obligation of prayer. The essential components (rukn) are the washing of limbs or the affected area, and the ruling (hukm) is that it permits acts otherwise forbidden without purification. The instruments are water, earth (tayammum), and their substitutes. Conditions are bifurcated into conditions for obligation (shurut al-wujub) and conditions for validity (shurut al-sihha), a distinction elaborated in longer texts. The commentator then justifies the author's use of the singular 'tahara' despite multiple types: it is a verbal noun (masdar) that covers all, and it is concise. Finally, he explains the precedence of the topic: because salah is the greatest pillar, and tahara is its condition, the condition is logically placed before the conditioned. Even within purification, wudu (minor) is placed before ghusl (major) because of greater general need and Qur'anic order.

Key Terms
الطهارة (al-tahara): Cleanliness from both actual impurity (najasa) and legal impurity (hadath), with instruments of water and earth.
شرائطها (shara'ituha): The conditions of tahara, divided into conditions of obligation (shurut al-wujub) and conditions of validity (shurut al-sihha).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is tahara placed before salah in fiqh books?
A) Because tahara is more important than salah.
B) Because tahara is a condition for salah, and the condition precedes the conditioned.
C) Because tahara is easier to learn than salah.
D) Because the author prefers to start with easier topics.

Key Takeaway
The placement of Kitab al-Tahara first in Hanafi fiqh works is not arbitrary; it follows the logical principle that conditions are presented before the acts they condition.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
15 min
The Four Obligatory Acts (Fara'id) of Wudu
Defines fard and wudu, lists four obligatory acts, defends washing of feet against jar reading.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four obligatory acts of wudu and identify their precise limits as defined in the Hanafi school, and explain the evidence for washing the feet and wiping a quarter of the head.

Core Idea
Fard in wudu is defined by Qur'anic imperative and prophetic bayan: four acts with precise boundaries.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq lays out the essential building blocks of wudu — the fara'id (obligatory acts) that must be fulfilled for wudu to be valid. It not only lists the four acts but defines each one with careful precision, from the limits of the face to the inclusion of elbows and ankles, and tackles a critical linguistic and hadith-based objection regarding the wiping versus washing of the feet. Understanding these definitions is the first step to performing correct wudu.

Guided Path
1
Define the term 'fard' and 'wudu'
Read the opening definitions. Note that 'fard' in Hanafi usage is a ruling from definitive evidence (qat'i), and 'wudu' linguistically and legally means cleanliness of specific limbs in a specific manner.
The passage distinguishes between qat'i fard and 'amali fard; wudu's three vowellings indicate different meanings.
2
Identify the four obligatory acts
Locate the sentence: 'غسل وجهه ... ويديه بمرفقيه ... ورجليه بكعبيه ... ومسح ربع رأسه'. These are the four fara'id: washing the face, hands (including elbows), feet (including ankles), and wiping a quarter of the head.
The order is important: face, then hands, then feet, then wiping head.
3
Parse the precise limits of each act
For each limb, find the boundary definitions: the face from hairline to chin and earlobe to earlobe; hands 'including elbows' with the preposition 'bi' meaning inclusion; feet 'including ankles' with similar reasoning; head wiping one quarter based on the hadith of al-Mughirah.
The author uses grammatical and lexical analysis to justify these limits, e.g., the meaning of 'ila' in the verse as 'ma'a'.
4
Analyze the evidence for washing the feet
Read the rebuttal of the jar reading (al-jarr bil-jiwar) and the citation of hadith from Ibn 'Umar and 'A'isha. Understand why the Hanafi school insists on washing the feet despite the variant qira'ah.
The argument: apparent meaning of jar is abandoned by consensus, and washing is proven by mutawatir hadith.
5
Evaluate the obligation of wiping a quarter of the head
Note the hadith of al-Mughira and the explanation that this khabar wahid is not an addition but a bayan (elaboration) of the mujmal Qur'anic command. Contrast with Shafi'i view that any part suffices.
The passage concludes that wiping a quarter is fard, refuting the view that minimal wiping suffices.

Source Text

فرض الوضوء أي فرض للوضوء، و"الفرض" في اللغة التقدير، وفي الشرع عبارة عن حكم مقدر لا يحتمل زيادة ولا نقصانا؛ لأنه ثبت بدليل قطعي لا شبهة فيه، وهذا تعريف للفرض القطعي دون العملي، والأولى أن يفسر الفرض بما لزم فعله؛ ليعم النوعين والوضوء" بضم الواو، مأخوذ من الوضاءة، وهي النظافة مطلقاً، وبفتح الواو اسم لما يحصل به النظافة، وبكسر الواو اسم للموضع الذي يتوضأ فيه، وفي الشرع: نظافة محل مخصوص وهو الأعضاء الأربعة على الوجه المخصوص الذي بينه الشارع في قوله: ﴿فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ (المائدة:1) فإن قيل: آية الوضوء مدنية بالاتفاق والصلاة فرضت بمكة، فيلزم كون الصلاة بلا وضوء إلى حين نزولها؟ قلنا: لا يلزم؛ لأنه يجوز أن يثبت الوضوء بالوحي الغير المتلو، والأخذ من الشرائع السابقة، كما حين توضأ ثلاثاً ثلاثاً، قال: "هذا وضوئي ووضوء الأنبياء من قبلي". (فتح) غسل وجهه وهو من قصاص شعره إلى أسفل ذقنه وإلى شحمي الأذن، ويديه بمرفقيه، ورجليه بكعبيه، ومسح ربع رأسه. وغسل وجهه: [هو إسالة الماء على المحل بحيث يتقاطر "الغسل": بالفتح إزالة الوسخ عن الشيء بإجراء الماء عليه، وبالضم اسم لغسل تمام الجسد وللماء الذي يغسل به وبالكسر ما يغسل به الرأس من خطمي ونحوه، وشرعاً: هو الإسالة، وحد الإسالة: أن يتقاطر الماء ولو قطرة أو قطرتين، وإضافة الغسل إلى الوجه إضافة المصدر إلى المفعول، والفاعل محذوف، ويدل عليه لفظ الوضوء، والضمير في وجهه راجع إلى ذلك الفاعل، يعني المتوضي وجهه. (فتح) قصاص شعره إلخ "القصاص" بتثليث القاف، والضم أولى أعلاها المنبت، و"الشعر" بإسكان العين وتحريكها ما ينبته الجسم مما ليس بصوف ولا وبر للإنسان وغيره، و"الذقن" بفتحتين من الإنسان مجتمع شعر اللحيين، وهذا بيان لحد الوجه طولاً، وحد الوجه عرضاً من شحمة الأذن إلى شحمة الأذن. (فتح) ويديه بمرفقيه أي مع مرفقيه آثر التعبير بها على "مع"؛ لأنها لابتداء المصاحبة، والباء لاستدامتها، والمرفق بكسر الميم وفتح الفاء، وفيه العكس لغة من الإنسان والدابة: أعلى الذراع وأفضل العضد، ستمي به؛ لأنه يرتفق به من الاتكاء ونحوه، وفيه إيماء إلى أن "إلى" في الآية بمعنى "مع". (فتح) ورجليه بكعبيه أي مع كعبيه، فإن قيل: قراءة الجر في أرجلكم" متواترة، ومقتضى الجمع بين القراءتين إما تخيير بين الغسل والمسح كما قال به الشيعة، أو يحمل النصب على حالة التحفي، والجر على حالة التخفف كما قال به بعضهم قلنا: قراءة الجر ظاهرها متروك بالإجماع؛ لأن من قال بالمسح لم يجعله مغيًا بالكعبين، فيكون الجر بالجوار كما في جحر ضب خرب"، ونظائره كثيرة في القرآن والشعر وغسل الرجلين ثابت إلينا بحديث الله بن عمر لما قال : رجعنا مع رسول الله الا الله من مكة إلى المدينة، حتى إذا كنا بماء في الطريق، فعجل قوم عند العصر، فتوضؤوا وهم عجال، فانتهينا إليهم وأعقابهم تلوح لم يمسها الماء، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: "ويل للأعقاب من النار، أسبغوا الوضوء"، رواه مسلم، وبحديث عائشة لها أنها قالت: إن القطع أحب إلي من أن أمسح على القدمين من غير خفين، فعلم أن القول بالمسح على الرجل العاري تفسق. (فتح) والمراد بالكعب ههنا العظم الناتئ أي المرتفع، لا كما رواه هشام عن محمد أنه المفصل الذي في وسط القدم عند معقد الشراك؛ لأن الكعب اسم للمفصل، ومنه كعوب الرمح؛ لأنهم ذكروا أن هذا سهو من هشام ولم يرو محمد تفسير الكعب بهذا في الطهارة، وإنما أراد في المُحرم إذا لم يجد نعلين أنه يقطع خفيه أسفل من كعبيه. (فتح، مسكين، غيرهما) ومسح ربع رأسه عطف على قوله: "غسل وجهه فيفيد العبارة فرضية مسح الربع، وذلك لحديث المغيرة بن شعبة أن النبي مسح بناصيته وعلى العمامة والخفين رواه مسلم وليس هذا بزيادة على الكتاب بخبر الواحد؛ لأن الكتاب مجمل، فالتحق الخبر بياناً، وهو حجة على الشافعي في تجويزه أدنى ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح، =
The fard of wudu, i.e. the obligatory (act) for wudu. 'Fard' linguistically means estimation, and legally it is a determined ruling that does not admit increase or decrease because it is established by a definitive evidence with no doubt. This is a definition of qat'i fard, not 'amali. It is better to define fard as what is binding to do, to include both types. 'Wudu' with damm on the waw, derived from wada'a which is cleanliness absolutely. With fatha on the waw, it is the name for what brings about cleanliness. With kasra on the waw, it is the name for the place where one performs wudu. Legally: cleanliness of specific parts, which are the four limbs, in the specific manner explained by the Lawgiver in His saying: 'Wash your faces...' (al-Ma'idah 5:6). If it is said: The verse of wudu is Madani by consensus, and salah was made obligatory in Makkah, so it would entail that salah existed without wudu until its revelation? We say: It does not necessarily follow, because it is permissible that wudu was established by non-recited revelation and by taking from previous laws, just as when he performed wudu three times, he said: 'This is my wudu and the wudu of the prophets before me.' (Fath) Washing his face, which is from the hairline (the top of the head where hair grows) to the bottom of his chin and to the earlobes, and his hands including the elbows, and his feet including the ankles, and wiping a quarter of his head. Washing his face: it is the flow of water over the area such that it drips. 'Ghusl' with fatha: removal of dirt from something by causing water to flow over it; with damm: name for washing the entire body and the water used; with kasra: what is used to wash the head such as soap etc. Legally: it is the flow [of water], and the minimum of flow is that water drips, even one or two drops. The genitive construction of 'ghusl' to 'face' is the addition of the verbal noun to the object; the subject is omitted, and it is indicated by the word 'wudu', and the pronoun in 'his face' refers back to that subject, i.e., the one performing wudu's face. (Fath) 'Of his hair...' etc. 'Al-qisas' with three possible vowels on the qaf, and damm is preferable: the topmost point of growth. 'Al-sha'r' with sukun on the 'ayn and with vowel: what the body grows besides wool and fur, for humans and others. 'Al-dhaqan' with two fatha: the meeting point of the two sides of beard hair. This is an explanation of the length limit of the face, and the width limit of the face from earlobe to earlobe. (Fath) 'And his hands including the elbows' i.e., with his elbows. The author preferred 'bi' over 'ma'a' because 'bi' indicates concomitant beginning, and 'ba' indicates its continuance. 'Al-mirfaq' with kasr on the meem and fatha on the fa', and the opposite is a dialect: from human and beast: the top of the forearm and the best of the upper arm, named because one leans on it for support etc. And in it is an indication that 'ila' in the verse means 'with'. (Fath) 'And his feet including his ankles' i.e., with his ankles. If it is said: The recitation with jar (genitive) in 'arjulakum' is mutawatir, and the requirement of harmonizing the two recitations is either a choice between washing and wiping as the Shi'a opine, or that the nasb is in the case of wearing shoes and the jar in the case of not wearing shoes as some say. We say: The apparent meaning of the jar recitation is abandoned by consensus; because those who said wiping did not make it limited by the ankles, so the jar is due to adjacency (jarr bil-jiwār) as in 'juhri dhabbi kharib' and many similar examples in the Qur'an and poetry. Washing the feet is established to us by the hadith of 'Abdullah ibn 'Umar: 'We returned with the Messenger of Allah from Makkah to Madinah. When we were at some water on the way, some people rushed for 'Asr prayer and performed wudu hastily. We reached them and their heels were dry, not touched by water. So the Messenger of Allah said, 'Woe to the heels from the Fire! Make wudu complete.''' Narrated by Muslim. And by the hadith of 'A'ishah who said: 'Amputation is more beloved to me than that I should wipe over the bare feet [without leather socks]', so it is known that the opinion of wiping the bare foot constitutes wickedness (tafsiq). (Fath) The intended meaning of 'ka'bi' here is the protruding bone, i.e., the raised one, not as Hisham narrated from Muhammad that it is the joint in the middle of the foot at the place of the shoelace, because ka'b is a name for the joint, from which comes 'ka'b of the spear'. Because they mentioned that this was a slip from Hisham, and Muhammad did not narrate the interpretation of ka'b with this in tahara; rather, he meant that for a muhrim who does not find sandals, he should cut his khuffs below the ankles. (Fath, Miskin, and others) 'And wiping a quarter of his head' is conjoined to 'washing his face', so the expression indicates the obligatory nature of wiping a quarter, because of the hadith of al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba that the Prophet wiped his forelock and over the turban and the khuffs. Narrated by Muslim. And this is not an addition to the Book by a solitary report (khabar wahid), because the Book is mujmal (summarized), so the report is attached as explanation (bayan). And it is a proof against al-Shafi'i in his allowing the minimum that can be called wiping. =
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a detailed parsing exercise on the fara'id (obligatory acts) of wudu. It begins by defining 'fard' linguistically and legally, distinguishing between qat'i (definitive) and 'amali (practical) fard, and then defines 'wudu' with its three possible vowellings. A critical objection is addressed: if the wudu verse was revealed in Madina but salah was obligated in Makka, how was salah performed without wudu? The answer: wudu could have been established by revelation other than the Qur'an (khabar wahid) and by following previous prophets' practices, as the Prophet's own wudu and that of previous prophets indicates. Then the four obligatory acts are listed: washing the face, washing the hands including elbows, washing the feet including ankles, and wiping one quarter of the head. The face is defined in length from hairline to chin bottom and in width from earlobe to earlobe. Washing (ghusl) is defined as flowing water such that it drips, even minimally. The hands include the elbows—the preposition 'bi' indicates inclusion, clarifying that 'ila' in the Qur'an means 'with'. The feet include the ankles; here, a lengthy rebuttal addresses the variant reading with genitive 'arjulikum', arguing it is due to adjacency (jarr bil-jiwar) and not an indication of wiping, citing strong hadith evidence for washing from Ibn 'Umar and 'A'isha. The ka'b is defined as the protruding ankle bone, refuting an alternate interpretation. Finally, wiping a quarter of the head is made obligatory by the hadith of al-Mughira, and the passage argues it is a valid explanation (bayan) of the Qur'anic command, not an addition, and it contradicts al-Shafi'i's view that a minimal wipe suffices.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): A ruling established by definitive evidence (dalil qat'i) that does not admit increase or decrease; also what is binding to do.
الوضوء (al-wudu): Cleanliness of specific limbs (face, hands, feet, wiping head) in a specific manner as prescribed by the Lawgiver.
الجار بالجوار (al-jarr bil-jiwar): A grammatical principle where a word takes the genitive case due to adjacency to a preceding genitive, not due to its own grammatical function.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the minimum required for wiping the head in wudu according to the Hanafi school?
A) Any amount that can be called wiping.
B) Wiping the entire head.
C) Wiping one quarter of the head.
D) Wiping the forelock only.

Key Takeaway
The four fara'id of wudu are washing the face and hands to elbows, wiping a quarter of the head, and washing the feet to ankles.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
8 min
The Five Obligations (Fara'id) of Wudu
Lists five mandatory acts with proofs and inter-school disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five fara'id of wudu according to the Hanafi school and describe the anatomical limits for each.

Core Idea
Wudu has five obligatory acts: washing face, hands, feet, and wiping a quarter of the head and beard.

This lesson presents the core obligations of wudu as defined by the Hanafi school. Understanding these fara'id is essential because omitting any one invalidates the wudu, which is a prerequisite for prayer. The text from Kanz al-Daqaiq lists each obligation with precise anatomical boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage begins by stating 'فرائض الوضوء' and then lists five acts: غسل وجهه, ويديه, ورجليه, ومسح ربع رأسه, and later mentions beard. Each is a fard (obligatory) and omitting any one invalidates the wudu.
The beard is mentioned later in the passage as part of the face or additional.
2
Define the limits of the face
The face extends from the hairline (قصاص شعر) to the bottom of the chin (أسفل ذقن) and from one ear lobe (شحمة أذن) to the other. This is the standard Hanafi definition.
The beard is considered part of the face for washing, but then later there is a separate obligation for wiping the beard.
3
Define washing of hands and feet
Hands must be washed including the elbows, and feet including the ankles. The preposition 'bi' in the text indicates inclusion ('with' the elbows/ankles).
The text explains that 'ila' in the Quran is interpreted as 'ma'a' (with).
4
Define wiping of the head
Wiping a quarter of the head is fard. The text specifies 'ربع رأسه' and later cites the hadith of al-Mughira as proof.
This distinguishes Hanafi from Shafi'i (any part) and Maliki (whole head).
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on beard
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider wiping a quarter of the beard as fard, while Abu Yusuf requires wiping all of it. The mufta bihi is the quarter.
The beard is defined as part of the face, but the wiping is additional.

فرائض الوضوء
غسل وجهه وهو من قصاص شعره إلى أسفل ذقنه وإلى شحمي الأذن، ويديه
أي الوجه
هذا حد الوجه طولاً
ف ك
بمرفقيه، ورجليه بكعبيه، ومسح ربع رأسه
وشحمة الأذن مالان منها
منتهى
غسل
غسل وجهه: [هو إسالة الماء على المحل بحيث يتقاطر "الغسل": بالفتح إزالة الوسخ عن الشيء بإجراء الماء عليه، وبالضم اسم لغسل تمام الجسد وللماء الذي يغسل به وبالكسر ما يغسل به الرأس من خطمي ونحوه، وشرعاً: هو الإسالة، وحد الإسالة: أن يتقاطر الماء ولو قطرة أو قطرتين، وإضافة الغسل إلى الوجه إضافة المصدر إلى المفعول، والفاعل محذوف، ويدل عليه لفظ الوضوء، والضمير في وجهه راجع إلى ذلك الفاعل، يعني المتوضي وجهه. (فتح) قصاص شعره إلخ "القصاص" بتثليث القاف، والضم أولى أعلاها المنبت، و"الشعر" بإسكان العين وتحريكها ما ينبته الجسم مما ليس بصوف ولا وبر للإنسان وغيره، و"الذقن" بفتحتين من الإنسان مجتمع شعر اللحيين، وهذا بيان لحد الوجه طولاً، وحد الوجه عرضاً من شحمة الأذن إلى شحمة الأذن. (فتح) ويديه بمرفقيه أي مع مرفقيه آثر التعبير بها على "مع"؛ لأنها لابتداء المصاحبة، والباء لاستدامتها، والمرفق بكسر الميم وفتح الفاء، وفيه العكس لغة من الإنسان والدابة: أعلى الذراع وأفضل العضد، ستمي به؛ لأنه يرتفق به من الاتكاء ونحوه، وفيه إيماء إلى أن "إلى" في الآية بمعنى "مع". (فتح) ورجليه بكعبيه أي مع كعبيه، فإن قيل: قراءة الجر في أرجلكم" متواترة، ومقتضى الجمع بين القراءتين إما تخيير بين الغسل والمسح كما قال به الشيعة، أو يحمل النصب على حالة التحفي، والجر على حالة التخفف كما قال به بعضهم قلنا: قراءة الجر ظاهرها متروك بالإجماع؛ لأن من قال بالمسح لم يجعله مغيًا بالكعبين، فيكون الجر بالجوار كما في جحر ضب خرب"، ونظائره كثيرة في القرآن والشعر وغسل الرجلين ثابت إلينا بحديث عبد الله بن عمر لما قال : رجعنا مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من مكة إلى المدينة، حتى إذا كنا بماء في الطريق، فعجل قوم عند العصر، فتوضؤوا وهم عجال، فانتهينا إليهم وأعقابهم تلوح لم يمسها الماء، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: "ويل للأعقاب من النار، أسبغوا الوضوء"، رواه مسلم، وبحديث عائشة رضي الله عنها أنها قالت: إن القطع أحب إلي من أن أمسح على القدمين من غير خفين، فعلم أن القول بالمسح على الرجل العاري تفسق. (فتح) والمراد بالكعب ههنا العظم الناتئ أي المرتفع، لا كما رواه هشام عن محمد أنه المفصل الذي في وسط القدم عند معقد الشراك؛ لأن الكعب اسم للمفصل، ومنه كعوب الرمح؛ لأنهم ذكروا أن هذا سهو من هشام ولم يرو محمد تفسير الكعب بهذا في الطهارة، وإنما أراد في المُحرم إذا لم يجد نعلين أنه يقطع خفيه أسفل من كعبيه. (فتح، مسكين، غيرهما) ومسح ربع رأسه عطف على قوله: "غسل وجهه فيفيد العبارة فرضية مسح الربع، وذلك لحديث المغيرة بن شعبة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مسح بناصيته وعلى العمامة، والخفين رواه مسلم وليس هذا بزيادة على الكتاب بخبر الواحد؛ لأن الكتاب مجمل، فالتحق الخبر بياناً، وهو حجة على الشافعي في تجويزه أدنى ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح، = عبد
كتاب الطهارة
ح مس
ولحيته.
Book of Purification 14 The Obligations of Wudu: Washing his face which is from the hairline to the bottom of his chin and to the ear lobes, and his hands (meaning the face, this is the length limit of the face) with his elbows, and his feet with his ankles, and wiping a quarter of his head. And the ear lobe is what is soft from it. (End) Washing of his face: it is pouring water on the place such that it drips. 'Ghusl' with fatha is removal of dirt from something by running water over it, with damm it is a noun for washing the entire body and for the water used for washing, with kasr it is what is used to wash the head like soap etc. Legally: it is pouring. The limit of pouring: that water drips even one or two drops. Adding 'washing' to 'his face' is addition of the masdar to the object, the subject omitted, and the pronoun in 'his face' refers to that subject, meaning the person performing wudu. (Fath) 'Hairline' etc: 'al-qisas' with three possible vowels for the qaf, damm is preferable meaning the top part where hair grows. 'al-sha'r' with sukun or tashriq the hair that grows on the body which is not wool or fur for humans and others. 'al-dhaqan' with two fatha is the meeting place of the beard on a human. This is an explanation of the length limit of the face. The width limit of the face is from one ear lobe to the other ear lobe. (Fath) 'And his hands with his elbows' meaning including his elbows. He preferred this expression over 'ma'a' because the ba implies continuous accompaniment. The elbow is the highest part of the forearm and the best of the upper arm, so named because one leans on it. This implies that 'ila' in the verse means 'with'. (Fath) 'And his feet with his ankles' meaning including his ankles. If it is said: the jar reading of 'arjulikum' is mutawatir, and the requirement of combining the two readings is either choice between washing and wiping as the Shia say, or carrying the nasb on the case of wearing shoes and jar on the case of being barefoot as some say, we say: the apparent jar reading is abandoned by consensus because those who say wiping did not make it limited to the ankles, so the jar is by neighborhood as in 'juhru dhahbin kharib', and its many parallels in Quran and poetry. Washing the feet is established to us by the hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar when he said: 'We returned with the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, from Mecca to Medina until when we were at a water source on the road, some people hastened at Asr, and they performed wudu quickly, and we came to them and their heels were shining, water had not touched them. So the Messenger of Allah said: 'Woe to the heels from the Fire, perform wudu properly.' Reported by Muslim. And by the hadith of Aisha that she said: 'I prefer cutting (the hand) over wiping the feet without socks.' So it is known that the saying of wiping the bare foot is sinful. (Fath) And the meaning of 'ka'b' here is the protruding bone, not as Hisham reported from Muhammad that it is the joint in the middle of the foot at the crossing of the shoelace, because 'ka'b' is a name for the joint, hence 'ka'ub' of the spear. They mentioned that this was an error from Hisham, and Muhammad did not report the interpretation of 'ka'b' in purification; rather he meant in the case of the muhrim who does not find sandals, that he should cut his slippers below his ankles. (Fath, Miskin, others) 'And wiping a quarter of his head' is connected to his saying 'washing his face', so the expression indicates the obligation of wiping the quarter. That is due to the hadith of al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba that the Prophet wiped his forelock and over the turban and the socks. Reported by Muslim. This is not an addition to the Book through a solo hadith because the Book is brief, so the hadith is explanatory, and it is a proof against al-Shafi'i who permits the minimum that can be called wiping.  =Abd Book of Purification Ha, Mim, And his beard.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Purification (Kitab al-Tahara) and immediately presents the obligations (fara'id) of wudu. It is a ruling_with_proof passage: it lists each fard and then provides textual evidence and addresses inter-school disputes. The author begins by stating the fara'id: washing the face (including the hairline to chin and between the ear lobes), washing the hands to the elbows, washing the feet to the ankles, and wiping a quarter of the head. The beard is added later as an additional fard according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The commentary then elaborates each term: 'ghusl' is defined legally as pouring water such that it drips; 'qisas' is the hairline; 'dhaqan' is the chin; the hand-washing includes the elbows, indicated by the preposition 'bi'; the foot-washing includes the ankles, using evidence from Quranic readings and hadith. A significant inter-school dispute is addressed regarding the feet: the Shia read the verse with jar (implying wiping), but the Hanafis argue this is due to grammatical adjacency, not an alternative ruling. They support washing with the hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar about heels and Aisha's statement. Another dispute concerns the head wiping: the Hanafis require a quarter, while al-Shafi'i accepts any part, and Maliki requires the whole head. The Hanafi proof is the hadith of al-Mughira. Finally, the beard is mentioned as requiring wiping of a quarter (or all according to Abu Yusuf).

Key Terms
الوضوء (al-wuḍū'): Ritual washing of specific body parts to remove minor impurity (hadath asghar).
الغسل (al-ghusl): Pouring water over a body part such that it flows or drips; legally the act of washing in wudu.
المسح (al-masḥ): Wiping; passing a wet hand over a body part; legally, wetting the part without flowing.
الكعب (al-ka'b): The protruding ankle bone on each foot; the limit for washing feet.
الربع (al-rubu'): One quarter; the minimum amount of head to be wiped.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum amount of the head that must be wiped during wudu?
A) Any part of the head
B) One quarter (rub')
C) The whole head
D) The forelock only

Key Takeaway
The five fara'id of wudu are washing the face, hands to elbows, feet to ankles, and wiping a quarter of the head and beard, with precise anatomical limits.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate

The Quranic Proof for Washing the Feet and Refutation of Wiping
Lists five mandatory acts with proofs and inter-school disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi proof for washing the feet in wudu and refute the Shia claim for wiping using the Quranic reading and hadith.

Core Idea
Washing the feet is fard; the jar reading is explained by adjacency, not an alternative ruling.

The Quranic verse on wudu has two readings for 'arjulikum' (your feet): one in the accusative (nasb) and one in the genitive (jar). The Shia use the jar reading to claim that wiping the feet is permissible instead of washing. This lesson delves into the Hanafi response, which relies on grammar, consensus, and hadith to affirm that washing the feet remains obligatory. Understanding this dispute clarifies why Hanafis insist on washing feet up to the ankles.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Hanafis hold that washing the feet including the ankles is fard. Wiping is not sufficient, even if the reading of the verse is in the genitive.
This is based on the explicit command in the verse and the Prophet's practice.
2
Identify the opposing proof
The Shia and some others cite the mutawatir reading of 'arjulikum' in the genitive (jar) as evidence for wiping.
They argue that the genitive indicates the feet are to be wiped like the head.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response: jar bil-jiwar
Hanafi scholars explain that the genitive is due to grammatical adjacency (jar bil-jiwar) to the preceding word 'ru'usikum' (your heads). This is a known phenomenon in Arabic, e.g., 'juhru dhahbin kharib'.
This explanation makes the jar reading consistent with the accusative reading which clearly indicates washing.
4
Cite hadith evidence
Hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar and Aisha explicitly show that the Prophet commanded washing the feet and warned against neglecting the heels. Aisha said she would prefer to have her hand cut than wipe the bare foot.
These hadith are decisive and show the practice of the Companions.
5
Conclude the dispute
The jar reading is not an alternative ruling; it is a grammatical variant. The consensus (ijma) of the Sahaba and the ongoing practice of Muslims supports washing. Therefore, wiping the feet is not valid.
The practical difference: Shia wipe feet, Hanafis wash feet.

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Purification (Kitab al-Tahara) and immediately presents the obligations (fara'id) of wudu. It is a ruling_with_proof passage: it lists each fard and then provides textual evidence and addresses inter-school disputes. The author begins by stating the fara'id: washing the face (including the hairline to chin and between the ear lobes), washing the hands to the elbows, washing the feet to the ankles, and wiping a quarter of the head. The beard is added later as an additional fard according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The commentary then elaborates each term: 'ghusl' is defined legally as pouring water such that it drips; 'qisas' is the hairline; 'dhaqan' is the chin; the hand-washing includes the elbows, indicated by the preposition 'bi'; the foot-washing includes the ankles, using evidence from Quranic readings and hadith. A significant inter-school dispute is addressed regarding the feet: the Shia read the verse with jar (implying wiping), but the Hanafis argue this is due to grammatical adjacency, not an alternative ruling. They support washing with the hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar about heels and Aisha's statement. Another dispute concerns the head wiping: the Hanafis require a quarter, while al-Shafi'i accepts any part, and Maliki requires the whole head. The Hanafi proof is the hadith of al-Mughira. Finally, the beard is mentioned as requiring wiping of a quarter (or all according to Abu Yusuf).

Key Terms
الجر بالجوار (al-jar bil-jiwar): Grammatical adjacency where a word takes the case of a neighboring word due to proximity, not intended meaning.
الكعب (al-ka'b): The protruding ankle bone; the limit for washing feet.
الغسل (al-ghusl): Washing with flowing water that drips.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi explanation for the genitive reading (jar) of 'arjulikum' in the wudu verse?
A) It is a weak reading and should be ignored
B) It is due to grammatical adjacency (jar bil-jiwar)
C) It indicates that wiping is an acceptable alternative
D) It applies only when wearing shoes

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that washing the feet is fard, using grammatical analysis and prophetic hadith to refute the wiping interpretation.

Taharah — Purification

The Obligation of Wiping the Head and Beard in Wudu
Lists five mandatory acts with proofs and inter-school disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi requirement of wiping a quarter of the head and beard, and compare it with other madhahib.

Core Idea
Wiping a quarter of the head is fard, and a quarter of the beard is also fard according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Wiping the head is one of the fara'id of wudu, but schools differ on the amount required. The Hanafi school requires a minimum of one quarter of the head, based on the hadith of al-Mughira and the principle that the Quranic command is general and the hadith specifies the minimum. Additionally, the beard must also be wiped (a quarter according to the majority, all according to Abu Yusuf). This lesson examines the evidence and the intra- and inter-madhhab differences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on head wiping
Hanafis require wiping a quarter of the head as fard. The text states 'مسح ربع رأسه' (wiping a quarter of his head).
This is the minimum; wiping the whole head is sunnah.
2
Present the proof
The proof is the hadith of al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba that the Prophet wiped his forelock (nasiyah) and over the turban and socks. Since the forelock is about a quarter of the head, it indicates the minimum.
The text argues this is not an addition to the Quran but an explanation (bayan).
3
Compare with other schools
Shafi'i: any part suffices (even a finger). Maliki: wiping the whole head is fard. Hanafi: quarter is fard, whole head is sunnah.
The practical difference: Shafi'i wipes a little, Maliki must cover the whole head, Hanafi wipes at least a quarter.
4
Address the beard wiping
The text mentions the beard at the end. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider wiping a quarter of the beard as fard (since it is part of the face), while Abu Yusuf requires the whole beard.
The mufta bihi is the quarter, but both are acknowledged.
5
Conclude the practical rule
In practice, a Hanafi should ensure that at least a quarter of the head and a quarter of the beard (if it exists) are wiped with wet hands.
The water does not need to flow; wetting is sufficient for masah.

(فتح) قصاص شعره إلخ "القصاص" بتثليث القاف، والضم أولى أعلاها المنبت، و"الشعر" بإسكان العين وتحريكها ما ينبته الجسم مما ليس بصوف ولا وبر للإنسان وغيره، و"الذقن" بفتحتين من الإنسان مجتمع شعر اللحيين، وهذا بيان لحد الوجه طولاً، وحد الوجه عرضاً من شحمة الأذن إلى شحمة الأذن. (فتح) ويديه بمرفقيه أي مع مرفقيه آثر التعبير بها على "مع"؛ لأنها لابتداء المصاحبة، والباء لاستدامتها، والمرفق بكسر الميم وفتح الفاء، وفيه العكس لغة من الإنسان والدابة: أعلى الذراع وأفضل العضد، ستمي به؛ لأنه يرتفق به من الاتكاء ونحوه، وفيه إيماء إلى أن "إلى" في الآية بمعنى "مع". (فتح) ورجليه بكعبيه أي مع كعبيه، فإن قيل: قراءة الجر في أرجلكم" متواترة، ومقتضى الجمع بين القراءتين إما تخيير بين الغسل والمسح كما قال به الشيعة، أو يحمل النصب على حالة التحفي، والجر على حالة التخفف كما قال به بعضهم قلنا: قراءة الجر ظاهرها متروك بالإجماع؛ لأن من قال بالمسح لم يجعله مغيًا بالكعبين، فيكون الجر بالجوار كما في جحر ضب خرب"، ونظائره كثيرة في القرآن والشعر وغسل الرجلين ثابت إلينا بحديث عبد الله بن عمر لما قال : رجعنا مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من مكة إلى المدينة، حتى إذا كنا بماء في الطريق، فعجل قوم عند العصر، فتوضؤوا وهم عجال، فانتهينا إليهم وأعقابهم تلوح لم يمسها الماء، فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: "ويل للأعقاب من النار، أسبغوا الوضوء"، رواه مسلم، وبحديث عائشة رضي الله عنها أنها قالت: إن القطع أحب إلي من أن أمسح على القدمين من غير خفين، فعلم أن القول بالمسح على الرجل العاري تفسق. (فتح) والمراد بالكعب ههنا العظم الناتئ أي المرتفع، لا كما رواه هشام عن محمد أنه المفصل الذي في وسط القدم عند معقد الشراك؛ لأن الكعب اسم للمفصل، ومنه كعوب الرمح؛ لأنهم ذكروا أن هذا سهو من هشام ولم يرو محمد تفسير الكعب بهذا في الطهارة، وإنما أراد في المُحرم إذا لم يجد نعلين أنه يقطع خفيه أسفل من كعبيه. (فتح، مسكين، غيرهما) ومسح ربع رأسه عطف على قوله: "غسل وجهه فيفيد العبارة فرضية مسح الربع، وذلك لحديث المغيرة بن شعبة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مسح بناصيته وعلى العمامة، والخفين رواه مسلم وليس هذا بزيادة على الكتاب بخبر الواحد؛ لأن الكتاب مجمل، فالتحق الخبر بياناً، وهو حجة على الشافعي في تجويزه أدنى ما يطلق عليه اسم المسح، = عبد
كتاب الطهارة
ح مس
ولحيته.
Book of Purification 14 The Obligations of Wudu: Washing his face which is from the hairline to the bottom of his chin and to the ear lobes, and his hands (meaning the face, this is the length limit of the face) with his elbows, and his feet with his ankles, and wiping a quarter of his head. And the ear lobe is what is soft from it. (End) Washing of his face: it is pouring water on the place such that it drips. 'Ghusl' with fatha is removal of dirt from something by running water over it, with damm it is a noun for washing the entire body and for the water used for washing, with kasr it is what is used to wash the head like soap etc. Legally: it is pouring. The limit of pouring: that water drips even one or two drops. Adding 'washing' to 'his face' is addition of the masdar to the object, the subject omitted, and the pronoun in 'his face' refers to that subject, meaning the person performing wudu. (Fath) 'Hairline' etc: 'al-qisas' with three possible vowels for the qaf, damm is preferable meaning the top part where hair grows. 'al-sha'r' with sukun or tashriq the hair that grows on the body which is not wool or fur for humans and others. 'al-dhaqan' with two fatha is the meeting place of the beard on a human. This is an explanation of the length limit of the face. The width limit of the face is from one ear lobe to the other ear lobe. (Fath) 'And his hands with his elbows' meaning including his elbows. He preferred this expression over 'ma'a' because the ba implies continuous accompaniment. The elbow is the highest part of the forearm and the best of the upper arm, so named because one leans on it. This implies that 'ila' in the verse means 'with'. (Fath) 'And his feet with his ankles' meaning including his ankles. If it is said: the jar reading of 'arjulikum' is mutawatir, and the requirement of combining the two readings is either choice between washing and wiping as the Shia say, or carrying the nasb on the case of wearing shoes and jar on the case of being barefoot as some say, we say: the apparent jar reading is abandoned by consensus because those who say wiping did not make it limited to the ankles, so the jar is by neighborhood as in 'juhru dhahbin kharib', and its many parallels in Quran and poetry. Washing the feet is established to us by the hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar when he said: 'We returned with the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, from Mecca to Medina until when we were at a water source on the road, some people hastened at Asr, and they performed wudu quickly, and we came to them and their heels were shining, water had not touched them. So the Messenger of Allah said: 'Woe to the heels from the Fire, perform wudu properly.' Reported by Muslim. And by the hadith of Aisha that she said: 'I prefer cutting (the hand) over wiping the feet without socks.' So it is known that the saying of wiping the bare foot is sinful. (Fath) And the meaning of 'ka'b' here is the protruding bone, not as Hisham reported from Muhammad that it is the joint in the middle of the foot at the crossing of the shoelace, because 'ka'b' is a name for the joint, hence 'ka'ub' of the spear. They mentioned that this was an error from Hisham, and Muhammad did not report the interpretation of 'ka'b' in purification; rather he meant in the case of the muhrim who does not find sandals, that he should cut his slippers below his ankles. (Fath, Miskin, others) 'And wiping a quarter of his head' is connected to his saying 'washing his face', so the expression indicates the obligation of wiping the quarter. That is due to the hadith of al-Mughira ibn Shu'ba that the Prophet wiped his forelock and over the turban and the socks. Reported by Muslim. This is not an addition to the Book through a solo hadith because the Book is brief, so the hadith is explanatory, and it is a proof against al-Shafi'i who permits the minimum that can be called wiping.  =Abd Book of Purification Ha, Mim, And his beard.

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Purification (Kitab al-Tahara) and immediately presents the obligations (fara'id) of wudu. It is a ruling_with_proof passage: it lists each fard and then provides textual evidence and addresses inter-school disputes. The author begins by stating the fara'id: washing the face (including the hairline to chin and between the ear lobes), washing the hands to the elbows, washing the feet to the ankles, and wiping a quarter of the head. The beard is added later as an additional fard according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. The commentary then elaborates each term: 'ghusl' is defined legally as pouring water such that it drips; 'qisas' is the hairline; 'dhaqan' is the chin; the hand-washing includes the elbows, indicated by the preposition 'bi'; the foot-washing includes the ankles, using evidence from Quranic readings and hadith. A significant inter-school dispute is addressed regarding the feet: the Shia read the verse with jar (implying wiping), but the Hanafis argue this is due to grammatical adjacency, not an alternative ruling. They support washing with the hadith of Abdullah ibn Umar about heels and Aisha's statement. Another dispute concerns the head wiping: the Hanafis require a quarter, while al-Shafi'i accepts any part, and Maliki requires the whole head. The Hanafi proof is the hadith of al-Mughira. Finally, the beard is mentioned as requiring wiping of a quarter (or all according to Abu Yusuf).

Key Terms
المسح (al-masḥ): Wiping; passing a wet hand over a body part.
الربع (al-rubu'): One quarter; the minimum amount of head to be wiped.
الناصية (al-nāṣiyah): Forelock; the front part of the hair on the head.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely requires a quarter of the head to be wiped, and a quarter of the beard, based on the hadith of al-Mughira and the principle of bayan.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate

The Sunnahs of Wuḍū': Recommended Acts and Their Proofs
Enumeration of the sunnah acts of wudu with proofs and an intra-Hanafi difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the sunnah acts of wudu according to the Hanafi school, state the evidence for siwak, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding khilal of the beard.

Core Idea
The sunnah acts of wudu complete its perfection; omitting them loses reward but not validity.

After establishing the obligatory (fard) acts of wudu, the Hanafi texts turn to the recommended (sunnah) acts that the Prophet consistently performed. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentary lists these sunan and provides evidence, particularly for siwak. It also highlights a dispute within the Hanafi school: is running wet fingers through the beard a sunnah or merely permissible? Understanding these distinctions is key to worshipping with knowledge and precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah acts listed
Scan the passage for the list: washing hands to wrists at the start, saying Bismillah, using siwak, rinsing mouth and nose, and performing khilal (wetting the beard and between fingers/toes). Note the textual order.
The list is not exhaustive; other schools may have additional sunan.
2
Analyze the proof for siwak
Find the hadith quoted: 'Lawla an ashaqqa 'ala ummati la-amartuhum bi al-siwak 'inda kulli wudu' (Were it not for hardship on my nation, I would have ordered them to use siwak with every wudu). Understand that the Prophet's consideration of hardship shows it is not obligatory, but his desire to command it shows strong recommendation, hence sunnah.
This is a sound hadith (Sunan Abu Dawud, etc.) and is the primary evidence.
3
Examine the dispute on beard khilal
The passage states: 'Wa khilal lihyatihi... qila sunnah 'inda Abi Yusuf, wa ja'iz 'indahuma' — meaning Abu Yusuf considers it sunnah, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad consider it merely permissible (ja'iz). Determine the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position: ja'iz.
The dispute is intra-Hanafi; the majority position is permissibility without labeling it sunnah.
4
Distinguish sunnah from wajib
The commentary defines sunnah as what the Prophet consistently did on a devotional basis, occasionally omitting. Contrast with wajib (omission is sinful) and mustahabb (not consistently done). The sunan in this passage are not required for validity; they perfect the act.
The hadith 'La wudu' li man lam yudhkir ism Allah' is interpreted as negation of virtue, not validity.
5
Apply the learning to practice
When performing wudu, incorporate these sunan consciously: start by washing hands, say Bismillah, use siwak if available, rinse mouth and nose thoroughly, and do khilal of fingers/toes. For the beard, it is permissible to do khilal but not required as sunnah; however, one may do it for extra care.
Regular practice of sunan increases reward and brings one closer to the Prophet's way.

سنن الوضوء
