Permissible Waters for Wudu and Effect of Pure Changes
Lists permissible water sources and rules when pure substances change the water's properties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wudu with water changed by a pure substance and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Water changed by a pure substance remains purifying for wudu — only impurity invalidates.

The Hanafi school permits wudu with water from rain, springs, and sea, even if a pure substance changes its colour, taste, or smell. This lesson explores the evidence and the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who restricts such water under certain conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled (wudu) and the legal status of its water sources: rainwater, spring water, and sea water are all permissible.
The ruling is that wudu is valid (sahih) with these waters.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites Quran 5:6 (general command to wash with water) and a hadith: 'Water is purifying, nothing makes it impure except what changes its colour, taste, or smell.' Identify these as the proofs.
The hadith is ahad (single-chain), so its thubut is zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The phrase 'لا ينجسه شيء طاهر' indicates that pure substances do not make water impure. The exception 'إلا ما غير' is interpreted as referring to an impure substance, not a pure one.
Thus a pure substance that changes water does not invalidate it for wudu.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that water changed by a non-earth substance like saffron becomes muqayyad (restricted water) and is invalid for wudu.
His proof: such water is no longer called 'water' absolutely; it is called 'saffron water'.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi response: attribution to saffron is for identification, not restriction; majority is by parts, not colour; the hadith's 'what changes' refers to impurity.
Thus the Hanafi position is mufta bihi and considered stronger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتوضأ بماء السماء والعين والبحر، وإن غيّر طاهر أحد أوصافه أو أنتن بالمكث؛ لقوله تعالى: {فاغسلوا وجوهكم} والغسل المعتاد بالماء المطلق، وإن غير شيء طاهر أحد أوصاف هذا الماء يعني لونه أو طعمه أو ريحه أو أنتن بطول المكث؛ لبقائه على وصف الطهارة؛ لقوله عليه: "الماء طهور لا ينجسه شيء طاهر إلا ما غير لونه أو طعمه أو ريحه". وفيه خلاف الشافعي فإنه يقول: لا يجوز التوضي بماء الزعفران وأشباهه مما ليس من جنس الأرض؛ لأنه ماء مقيد يقال له: ماء الزعفران، بخلاف أجزاء الأرض، فإن الماء لا يخلو عنها عادة. ولنا أن الإضافة إلى الزعفران كالإضافة إلى البئر والعين للتعريف لا للتقييد، والخلط القليل لا اعتبار له فيعتبر الغالب، والغلبة بالأجزاء لا بتغير اللون، والمراد من "ما غير" في الحديث شيء نجس. (مستخلص)
And one performs wudu with rainwater, spring water, and sea water, even if a pure thing changes one of its properties or it becomes putrid by staying. Because of His statement: {Wash your faces} and the customary washing is with absolute water. And if a pure thing changes one of the properties of this water, meaning its colour, taste, or smell, or it becomes putrid by long staying, (it is still permissible) because it remains with the description of purity. Due to his (peace be upon him) statement: 'Water is purifying, nothing makes it impure except what changes its colour, taste, or smell.' There is a dispute with al-Shafi'i, for he says: it is not permissible to perform wudu with water of saffron and its likes that are not from the category of earth, because it is restricted water, called saffron water, unlike parts of the earth, for water is not free from them customarily. And for us (Hanafi) the attribution to saffron is like the attribution to a well or spring, for identification not restriction. A small mixture is not considered; rather the majority is considered, and majority is by parts not by change of colour. And the meaning of 'what changes' in the hadith is something impure. (Extract)
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It begins by stating that wudu is valid with three types of water: rainwater (from the sky), spring water, and sea water. Even if a pure substance (tahir) alters one of the water's three properties (colour, taste, smell), or the water becomes stale and putrid due to prolonged storage, the water remains permissible for wudu. The proof is Quran 5:6 'Wash your faces', where the customary washing is done with absolute (mutlaq) water, and the hadith: 'Water is purifying and nothing renders it impure except what changes its colour, taste, or smell.' Here the hadith is interpreted to mean that only a substance that is inherently impure (najis) can render water impure by changing its properties. Pure substances do not remove the water's purifying quality. Then the passage introduces a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i: he disallows wudu with water mixed with saffron (and similar substances not of the earth's soil) because such water becomes restricted (muqayyad) and is called 'saffron water'. He analogizes earth's soil as an exception because water naturally contains earth particles. The Hanafi response (for us) is that attaching the name 'saffron water' is merely for identification (ta'rif), not restriction (taqyid), similar to saying 'well water' or 'spring water'. A small amount of mixture is negligible; the criterion is the majority component (ghalaba) in terms of parts (ajza'), not colour. The hadith's phrase 'what changes' refers to an impure thing, not a pure one. Thus the Hanafi school maintains that water mixed with a pure substance remains purifying as long as the water is still predominantly water.

Key Terms
ماء مطلق (ma' mutlaq): Absolute or pure water that is not mixed with any substance that would remove its name 'water'.
ماء مقيد (ma' muqayyad): Water that is mixed with a substance to the extent that it is no longer called 'water' absolutely, e.g., rose water, saffron water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, is it permissible to perform wudu with water whose colour has changed due to saffron being added?
A) Yes, it is permissible as long as the water remains mostly water by weight.
B) No, because saffron water is muqayyad and invalid for wudu.
C) Yes, but only if the saffron is removed.
D) No, because any colour change renders water impure.

Key Takeaway
Pure substances do not invalidate wudu water; only impure substances that change its properties do.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Water Changed by Leaves, Cooking, Extraction, and Overpowering Mixtures
Elaborates on when water becomes muqayyad, with harmonization of Hanafi opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criteria for water becoming muqayyad and identify whether a given mixture is valid for wudu.

Core Idea
Water for wudu becomes invalid if the mixture overcomes it by parts or completely mixes.

Wudu requires absolute (mutlaq) water, but everyday situations involve mixtures with leaves, cooking, or extraction. How do we determine if the water remains mutlaq? This lesson presents the Hanafi criteria and reconciles the various statements of the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal ruling
The passage states that wudu is not permissible with water that has become muqayyad (restricted). First, identify the act (wudu) and the condition (water must be mutlaq).
Muqayyad water is still pure (tahir) and can be drunk, but not used for wudu.
2
List the categories of muqayyad water
The passage covers four categories: water changed by many leaves, water changed by cooking (e.g., broth), water extracted from trees/fruits, and water overcome by another substance.
Each category has its own test: for solids, fluidity; for liquids, attribute change and weight dominance.
3
Apply the unified criteria
The passage harmonizes differing Hanafi views. The key principle: water remains mutlaq as long as the name 'water' applies. Restriction occurs either by complete mixing (cooking/absorption) or by the mixture overcoming by parts.
For solids, test fluidity (riqqa wa sayalan). For liquids, check if the mixture differs in all, some, or no attributes.
4
Determine the operative ruling
From the unified criteria, derive whether a given mixture is valid for wudu. The passage provides examples: grapevine sap without pressing is valid; milk that dominates in colour or taste is not.
This is a definition_ruling, so the outcome is a classification, not a dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بماء إلخ: أي لا يجوز الوضوء بماء تغير لونه بكثرة الأوراق الواقعة فيه، لكن يجوز شربه وغسل الأشياء به؛ لأنه طاهر، وأما عدم جواز التوضي به؛ لأنه غلب عليه الأوراق وصار مقيدا كماء الباقلى، وعلله في النهر الفائق بزوال اسم الماء عنه. تغيّر بأن خرج عن رقته وسيلانه. أو بالطبخ إلخ أي لا يتوضأ بماء تغير بسبب الطبخ بخلط طاهر كالمرق والباقلى؛ لأنه لم يبق ماء مطلقا فلم يتناوله النص، وكذا الحكم في ماء اعتصر من شجر أو ثمر ؛ لأنه ليس بماء مطلق، وفي ذكر العصر إشارة إلى أن ما يخرج من الشجر بلا عصر كماء يسيل من الكرم يجوز به الوضوء، وهو قول بعض المشايخ. وقوله: "أو غلب عليه غيره" أي لا يجوز التوضي بماء غلب عليه غير الماء من جهة الأجزاء، وهو احتراز عن الغلبة لونا وهو قول محمد؛ لزوال اسم الماء عن المخلوط بالغالب. واعلم أن عبارات الأصحاب قد اختلفت في هذا الباب مع اتفاقهم على أن المطلق يجوز استعماله، وما ليس مطلق لا يجوز، فمنهم من اعتبر الرقة والسيلان ومنهم من منع بتغير وصف، ومنهم من اعتبر تغير وصفين فأكثر، ومنهم من اعتبر الغلبة بالأجزاء، فلابد من ضابط موفق بين الأقوال، يحمل كل قول على ما يليق، فنقول: الماء إذا بقي على أصل خلقته ولم يزل عنه اسم الماء جاز الوضوء به، وإن زال وصار مقيداً لم يجز. والتقييد بأحد الأمرين إما بكمال الامتزاج أو بغلبة الممتزج، وكمال الامتزاج إما بطبخ بعد الخلط بشيء طاهر لا يقصد به المبالغة في التنظيف كالصابون أو بتشرب النبات، وغلبة الممتزج تكون بالاختلاط من غير طبخ ولا تشرب نبات، ثم المخالط للماء إما جامد أو مائع، فإن كان جامداً فالعبرة لبقاء الرقة والسيلان، فما دام رقيقاً يجري على الأعضاء يجوز استعماله، وإن كان مائعاً فإما أن يخالف الماء في الأوصاف كلها أو بعضها أو لا يخالف أصلاً، فإن لم يخالف كالماء المستعمل وكماء الورد المنقطع الرائحة، فالعبرة للغلبة أجزاء، فإن كانت الغلبة للمطلق من حيث الوزن جاز الاستعمال، وإن كان بالعكس لا يجوز، وإن خالفه في الأوصاف كلها فالعبرة في المنع لتغير الأوصاف كلها أو أكثرها، وإن خالفه في البعض كاللبن المخالف في اللون والطعم تعتبر الغلبة من ذلك الوجه، فإن غلب لون اللبن أو طعمه امتنع الجواز وإلا فلا. (فتح ملخصاً)
‘Not with water changed...’ etc., meaning: it is not permissible to perform wudu with water whose colour has changed due to many leaves falling into it, but it is permissible to drink it and wash things with it because it is pure. The reason for prohibition of wudu is that the leaves have overcome it and it has become restricted like water of broad beans. And al-Nahr al-Faiq explained it by the removal of the name ‘water’ from it. Change that removes its thinness and fluidity. Or due to cooking, etc., meaning one does not perform wudu with water changed due to cooking by mixing with a pure substance like broth or broad bean soup, because it no longer remains absolute water, thus the textual ruling does not apply. Similarly for water pressed from trees or fruits, because it is not absolute water. And the mention of pressing indicates that what comes out of a tree without pressing, like water flowing from grapevines, wudu is permissible with it, and this is the view of some shaykhs. And his statement: ‘or another substance overcomes it’, meaning wudu is not permissible with water that is overcome by something other than water in terms of parts, and this is a distinction from being overcome in colour only, and this is the view of Muhammad – because the name ‘water’ is removed from the mixture by the predominant. Know that the expressions of the companions have differed in this chapter despite their agreement that absolute water is permissible to use and what is not absolute is not permissible. Among them are those who consider fluidity and flow, some who prohibit with change of one attribute, some who consider change of two or more attributes, and some who consider overcoming by parts. Therefore a reconciling principle is needed, applying each statement appropriately. We say: water when it remains on its original creation and the name ‘water’ is not removed from it, wudu is permissible; if it is removed and it becomes restricted, it is not permissible. Restriction occurs in one of two ways: either by complete mixing or by the mixture overcoming. Complete mixing is either by cooking after mixing with a pure substance that is not intended for intense cleaning like soap, or by absorption by plants. Overcoming of the mixture occurs by mixing without cooking or plant absorption. Then the mixture with water is either solid or liquid. If solid, the criterion is the retention of thinness and flow; as long as it is thin and flows on the limbs, its use is permissible. If liquid, then either it differs from water in all attributes, some, or not at all. If not differing, like used water or rose water with no smell, the criterion is overcoming by parts: if the absolute (water) dominates in weight, use is permissible; if vice versa, not permissible. If it differs in all attributes, the criterion for prohibition is the change of all or most attributes. If it differs in some, like milk which differs in colour and taste, the overcoming in that respect is considered: if the colour or taste of milk dominates, wudu is prohibited; otherwise not. (Fath, summarized)
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This is a definition_ruling passage. It details cases where water becomes muqayyad and thus invalid for wudu. The commentator first explains the sub-headings from the matn: 'لا بماء' refers to water whose colour is changed by many leaves; such water is pure (tahir) and can be drunk, but wudu is invalid because the leaves have overcome it, making it muqayyad like broad bean water. Similarly, water changed by cooking with pure substances (broth, bean soup) loses the name 'water' entirely. Water pressed from trees or fruits (e.g., date sap) is also muqayyad. However, water that flows without pressing (like sap from a vine stem) is an exception; some mashayikh permit wudu with it. The passage then addresses 'or another substance overcomes it' meaning when a substance other than water predominates by parts (ajza'), not just by colour change. This is the view of Imam Muhammad. The commentator then provides a harmonization of several differing opinions among Hanafi scholars regarding the criterion for water being mutlaq. The unified principle: water is permissible for wudu as long as it remains on its original state and retains the name 'water'. Restriction occurs either by complete mixing (e.g., cooking with soap or absorption by plants) or by the mixture overcoming (by weight, not colour). For solid mixtures, fluidity is the test; for liquid mixtures, consider whether the mixture differs in all, some, or none of the three attributes. If no attribute change, the criterion is weight dominance. If all attributes change, prohibition is based on change of all or most. If only some change (e.g., milk), the overcoming in that specific attribute (colour/taste) prevents wudu.

Key Terms
غلب عليه غيره (ghalaba 'alayhi ghayruh): When a substance other than water overcomes water in quantity (by parts), rendering it muqayyad.
الرقة والسيلان (ar-riqqa wa as-sayalan): Thinness and fluidity; a test for whether a solid mixture remains water (if it flows on limbs, it's mutlaq).
كمال الامتزاج (kamal al-imtizaj): Complete mixing, e.g., by cooking a pure substance into water, resulting in restriction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the harmonized Hanafi view in the passage, which type of mixture is valid for wudu?
A) Water mixed with broth until it is thick and no longer flows
B) Water with a small amount of grape juice that does not change taste or colour
C) Water pressed from dates where the water is no longer fluid
D) Water with many leaves that changes colour but remains thin

Key Takeaway
Water becomes muqayyad (invalid for wudu) either by complete mixing (e.g., cooking) or by the mixture overcoming by parts; for solids, fluidity is the test, and for liquids, attribute dominance or weight dominance decides.

Water Mixed with Najasah and the Minimum Pool Size
Ruling on still water defilement with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on still water defilement, explain the Hanafi proof, and contrast it with the Shafi'i and Maliki positions.

Core Idea
Still water is defiled by najasah unless it is at least ten by ten cubits — even if unchanged.

In this passage, you will encounter a classical water-purity debate. The Hanafi school sets a minimum pool size of ten cubits by ten cubits; if the pool is smaller, it becomes impure upon contact with najasah, even if its attributes do not change. This ruling is contested by the Shafi'i and Maliki schools, each relying on different hadiths. Let's examine the evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: still water becomes impure (najis) when najasah (e.g., urine) enters, unless the pool is at least ten cubits by ten cubits. If it is that large, it remains pure unless the attributes change.
The term 'najs' with fat-ha on jim means the actual impure substance.
2
Identify the opponents and their views
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that water is pure if it reaches two qullas (about 500 ratls) regardless of size. Imam Malik holds that water remains pure unless its colour, taste, or smell change.
Each school uses a different threshold: volume (Shafi'i), attributes (Maliki), or area (Hanafi).
3
Follow the opponents' proofs
Shafi'i cites the hadith: 'When water reaches two qullas, it does not carry impurity.' Maliki cites: 'Allah created water purifying, nothing makes it impure except what changes it.'
These are the primary textual proofs for their positions.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafi relies on two stronger proofs: the Prophet's prohibition against urinating in still water, and the hadith about not dipping hand into a vessel before washing thrice. The Maliki hadith is interpreted as referring to flowing water (to harmonize), and the Shafi'i hadith is declared weak by scholars like al-Bayhaqi and al-Ghazali.
Thus, the Hanafi evidence is considered more robust.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: For a small pool (e.g., 8x8 cubits), Hanafi says it becomes impure by urine; Shafi'i says it is pure if volume exceeds two qullas; Maliki says pure unless attributes change.
This affects every wudu and ghusl performed with that water.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبماء دائم فيه نجس إن لم يكن عشراً في عشر، وإلا فهو نجس أي مطلقا سواء تغير أحد أوصافه أو لا، و"النجس" بفتح الجيم: عين النجاسة، وبكسرها: ما لا يكون طاهراً، والمراد هنا الأول. (مسكين). عشراً في عشر أي عشرة أذرع في عشرة أذرع، وقال الشافعي: يجوز إن كان قلتين، وهما خمس مائة رطل؛ لقوله عليه: "إذا بلغ الماء قلتين لم يحمل الخبث"، وقال مالك: يتوضأ به ما لم يتغير أحد أوصافه؛ لقوله عليه: "خلق الله الماء طهوراً لا ينجسه شيء إلا ما غير"، الحديث، ولنا: نهيه عليه السلام عن البول في الماء الدائم، وعن غمس اليد في الإناء قبل أن يغسلها ثلاثاً، وما رواه الإمام مالك محمول على الماء الجاري توفيقاً بين الأحاديث؛ لأنه ورد في بئر بضاعة وماؤها كان جارياً في البساتين، وحديث القلتين ضعفه جماعة من المحدثين، حتى قال البيهقي من الشافعية: الحديث غير قوي، وقد تركه الغزالي والرؤياني مع شدة اتباعهما للشافعي لضعفه، فلا يعارض ما روينا. (فتح). وإلا فهو إلخ: أي وإن لم يكن كذلك، إن كان عشراً في عشر، وقدّر عامة المشايخ العشر في العشر في الماء الدائم بذراع المساحة، وقيل: بذراع الكرباس توسعة للأمر على الناس؛ لأنه أقصر من ذراع المساحة؛ لأن ذراع الكرباس سبع مشتات ليس فوق كل مشت إصبع قائمة، وذراع المساحة سبع مشتات فوق كل مشت إصبع قائمة، والصحيح في العمق أن يكون بحال لا يظهر ما تحته بالاغتراف وقدره بعضهم بأربعة أصابع مفتوحة، هذا إذا كان الحوض مربعاً، فإن كان مدوراً، قيل: يعتبر أن يكون حول الماء ثمانية وأربعون ذراعاً، وقيل: ستة وثلاثون ذراعاً وهو الصحيح. (مسكين)
And with still water in which there is urine, if it is not ten by ten [cubits], then it is impure. i.e., absolutely, whether one of its attributes changed or not. And 'al-najs' with fat-ha on jim: the substance of impurity; with kasr: that which is not pure; here the first is intended. (Miskin). Ten by ten, i.e., ten cubits by ten cubits. And al-Shafi'i said: it is permissible if it is two qullas, which is five hundred ratls, due to his (peace be upon him) saying: 'When water reaches two qullas, it does not carry impurity.' And Malik said: one performs wudu with it as long as none of its attributes change, due to his saying: 'Allah created water purifying, nothing makes it impure except what changes it.' And for us: his prohibition (peace be upon him) against urinating in still water, and against dipping the hand in a vessel before washing it three times. And what Imam Malik narrated is interpreted as referring to flowing water, to harmonize the hadiths; because it was about the well of Buda'ah and its water was flowing in orchards. And the hadith of two qullas has been declared weak by a group of muhaddithin, so much so that al-Bayhaqi from the Shafi'iyyah said: the hadith is not strong. And al-Ghazali and al-Ruyani left it despite their strong adherence to al-Shafi'i due to its weakness. So it does not contradict what we have narrated. (Fath). And otherwise it is... i.e., if it is not like that, i.e., if it is ten by ten, then it is pure. And the general mashayikh estimated the ten by ten for still water by the surveying cubit (dira' al-misaha). And it is said: by the carpenter's cubit (dira' al-kirbas) to make things easier for people, because it is shorter. The correct depth is such that the bottom is not visible when scooping, and some estimated it as four open fingers. This if the basin is square. If it is round, it is said that the circumference should be forty-eight cubits, and it is said: thirty-six cubits, which is the correct view. (Miskin)
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute. The passage rules that if urine (or any najasah) falls into still water (ma' da'im, i.e., non-flowing pool), the entire body of water becomes impure unless the pool measures at least ten cubits by ten cubits (an area of 100 square cubits). In that case, the water remains pure despite the urine, provided none of its attributes (colour, taste, smell) change. The text then clarifies the pronunciation of 'najs' (with fatha on jim) meaning the actual impure substance, as opposed to 'najis' (with kasra) meaning something impure. It then presents differing views: Imam al-Shafi'i sets the threshold at two qullas (about 500 ratls, approximately 227 litres) based on the hadith: 'When water reaches two qullas, it does not carry impurity.' Imam Malik considers it purely based on change of properties: as long as the water's attributes are unchanged, it remains pure, citing the hadith: 'Allah created water purifying, nothing makes it impure except what changes it.' The Hanafi school (لنا) responds with two proofs: (1) The Prophet's prohibition against urinating in still water, and (2) the hadith about not dipping a hand into a vessel before washing it three times. These indicate that still water can be defiled by the mere presence of impurity, regardless of attribute change. The hadith used by Malik (about the well of Buda'ah) is interpreted to refer to flowing water, thus harmonizing. The Shafi'i hadith of two qullas is criticized as weak by many scholars, including al-Bayhaqi (a Shafi'i) and al-Ghazali, so it cannot outweigh the Hanafi evidence. The passage then details the measurement: ten by ten cubits using the surveying cubit (about 2.5 meters?); the carpenter's cubit is shorter and some allow it for convenience. Depth: shall allow the bottom to appear? Actually 'enough that the bottom is not visible when scooping' – some set it at four open fingers. For a round pool, the perimeter is either 48 or 36 cubits (the latter being correct).

Key Terms
ماء دائم (ma' da'im): Still, non-flowing water, such as a pond or cistern.
القلة (al-qulla): A large earthen jar; used as a unit of volume. Two qullas = 500 ratls.
ذراع المساحة (dira' al-misaha): Surveying cubit, used for land measurement. Longer than a carpenter's cubit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens when urine falls into a still pool that measures exactly 8 cubits by 8 cubits?
A) The entire pool becomes impure, and wudu cannot be performed with it.
B) It remains pure as long as the urine does not change the water's colour.
C) It is not impure because the pool is more than two qullas.
D) It is makruh but still permissible to use for wudu.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires still water to be at least ten-by-ten cubits to avoid defilement by najasah; smaller pools become impure regardless of attribute change.

Criteria for Water Mixed with Pure Substances
Reconciling criteria for mixed water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criteria to determine whether water mixed with a pure substance remains mutlaq and permissible for wudu.

Core Idea
Water mixed with a pure substance is permissible only if it retains the name 'water' — criteria vary by solid/liquid and attributes.

In this passage, the author reconciles the differing statements of Hanafi scholars by providing a comprehensive framework. You will learn the precise conditions under which water mixed with pure substances remains permissible for wudu, based on the nature of the mixture and the properties of the added substance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
Understand that all Hanafi scholars agree on the basic rule: mutlaq (unrestricted) water is permissible for wudu, while muqayyad (restricted) water is not. The challenge is determining when water becomes muqayyad.
The passage begins by noting differences among the companions (as'hab) but affirms the shared foundation.
2
Recognize the Two Ways Water Becomes Muqayyad
Water becomes restricted (muqayyad) either by complete mixing (kammal al-imitizaj) or by predominance (ghalabat al-mumtazaj) of the added substance. Complete mixing occurs through cooking or plant absorption; predominance occurs through simple mixing.
This is the author’s reconciling framework.
3
Distinguish Between Solid and Liquid Mixtures
The criterion differs based on whether the added substance is solid (jamid) or liquid (ma'i'). For solids, look at fluidity; for liquids, look at attributes and weight.
The passage explicitly separates these two categories.
4
Apply the Solid Criterion: Fluidity and Flow
For solid mixtures, permissibility depends on whether the water retains its thinness and ability to flow (al-riqqah wa al-sayalan). As long as the mixture is fluid and flows over the limbs, it is permissible.
This is a simple, observable test.
5
Apply the Liquid Criterion: Attributes and Predominance
For liquid mixtures, check whether the added liquid differs from water in its attributes (color, taste, smell). If no difference (e.g., used water or rose water without scent), predominance by weight (parts) decides. If all attributes differ, change in most or all attributes prohibits. If only some differ, predominant attribute in that specific aspect matters.
The author gives the example of milk: if milk's color or taste dominates, the mixture becomes muqayyad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن عبارات الأصحاب قد اختلفت في هذا الباب مع اتفاقهم على أن المطلق يجوز استعماله، وما ليس مطلق لا يجوز ، فمنهم من اعتبر الرقة والسيلان ومنهم من منع بتغير وصف، ومنهم من اعتبر تغير وصفين فأكثر، ومنهم من اعتبر الغلبة بالأجزاء، فلابد من ضابط موفق بين الأقوال، يحمل كل قول على ما يليق، فنقول: الماء إذا بقي على أصل خلقته ولم يزل عنه اسم الماء جاز الوضوء به، وإن زال وصار مقيداً لم يجز. والتقييد بأحد الأمرين إما بكمال الامتزاج أو بغلبة الممتزج، وكمال الامتزاج إما بطبخ بعد الخلط بشيء طاهر لا يقصد به المبالغة في التنظيف كالصابون أو بتشرب النبات، وغلبة الممتزج تكون بالاختلاط من غير طبخ ولا تشرب نبات، ثم المخالط للماء إما جامد أو مائع، فإن كان جامداً فالعبرة لبقاء الرقة والسيلان، فما دام رقيقاً يجري على الأعضاء يجوز استعماله، وإن كان مائعاً فإما أن يخالف الماء في الأوصاف كلها أو بعضها أو لا يخالف أصلاً، فإن لم يخالف كالماء المستعمل وكماء الورد المنقطع الرائحة، فالعبرة للغلبة أجزاء، فإن كانت الغلبة للمطلق من حيث الوزن جاز الاستعمال، وإن كان بالعكس لا يجوز، وإن خالفه في الأوصاف كلها فالعبرة في المنع لتغير الأوصاف كلها أو أكثرها، وإن خالفه في البعض كاللبن المخالف في اللون والطعم تعتبر الغلبة من ذلك الوجه، فإن غلب لون اللبن أو طعمه امتنع الجواز وإلا فلا. (فتح ملخصاً)
Know that the statements of the companions have differed in this chapter, despite their agreement that pure (mutlaq) water is permissible to use, and that which is not pure is not permissible. Some considered fluidity and flow, some prohibited due to change in one attribute, some considered change in two or more attributes, and some considered predominance in parts. So a comprehensive principle reconciling the statements is needed, each statement being applied appropriately. We say: Water, if it remains in its original creation and the name 'water' is not removed from it, then wudu with it is permissible. If it changes and becomes restricted (muqayyad), it is not permissible. Restriction occurs by either complete mixing or predominance of the mixed substance. Complete mixing is either by cooking after mixing with a pure substance not intended for excessive cleaning, like soap, or by absorption by plants. Predominance of the mixed substance occurs by mixing without cooking or absorption. Then, the substance mixed with water is either solid or liquid. If solid, the criterion is the preservation of fluidity and flow; as long as it is thin and flows over the limbs, its use is permissible. If liquid, then it either differs from water in all attributes, some, or not at all. If it does not differ, like used water or rose water that has lost its scent, the criterion is predominance by parts; if the pure water predominates by weight, use is permissible; if the opposite, it is not. If it differs in all attributes, the criterion for prohibition is change in all or most attributes. If it differs in some, like milk differing in color and taste, predominance in that respect is considered; if the color or taste of milk predominates, permissibility is prevented; otherwise, it is not. (Fath, summarized)
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a ruling providing a comprehensive framework for determining when water mixed with a pure substance remains pure and permissible for wudu. The author notes that Hanafi scholars have expressed various criteria, but all agree that only mutlaq (unrestricted) water is permissible. The passage reconciles these views by offering a detailed breakdown: water on its original state is permissible; if it becomes restricted (muqayyad), it is not. Restriction occurs by complete mixing (cooking or plant absorption) or by predominance (simple mixing). For solids, the key is fluidity; for liquids, it depends on whether it differs from water in attributes. If no difference, parts matter; if all attributes differ, change matters; if some, the specific attribute that changed matters. This reasoning is based on the general Hanafi principle of istihsan and practical necessity, aiming for a balanced criterion.

Key Terms
المطلق (al-mutlaq): Unrestricted water, pure and permissible for use.
المقيد (al-muqayyad): Restricted water, not permissible for wudu due to change from original state.
الرقة والسيلان (al-riqqah wa al-sayalan): Thinness and flow, indicating fluidity; the criterion for solid mixtures.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if water is mixed with a solid pure substance like saffron, what is the primary criterion for determining whether the mixture remains permissible for wudu?
A) Change in color
B) Predominance by weight
C) Preservation of fluidity and flow
D) The substance used

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of water mixed with a pure substance depends on specific Hanafi criteria that balance textual differences and practical necessity.

Purity of Large Standing Water: The Hanafi Position and Its Proofs
Stating the Hanafi position on large water bodies and refuting the qullatayn and absolute change views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on when large standing water becomes impure, identify the Shafi'i and Maliki proofs, and apply the Hanafi response to a similar case.

Core Idea
A large pond stays pure unless it changes; the qullatayn hadith is weak and Malik's hadith applies only to flowing water.

This passage presents a classic Hanafi-Shafi'i-Maliki dispute over when standing water becomes impure. The Hanafi school sets a clear size threshold (10×10 cubits) beyond which only a change in attributes matters. You will see how the Hanafi school defends its position by critiquing the proofs of the other schools and reconciling conflicting hadiths.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
State the ruling: a pond of standing water that is ten cubits by ten cubits does not become impure simply by the fall of an impurity. Only if one of its attributes (color, taste, smell) changes does it become impure. If smaller, it becomes impure regardless of change.
2
Identify the opponents and their views
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that water remains pure if it is two qullas (500 ratls) in volume, based on the hadith 'When water reaches two qullas, it does not carry impurity.' Imam Malik holds that any quantity remains pure unless an attribute changes, based on the hadith 'Allah created water as purifying; nothing makes it impure except what changes it.'
3
Examine the opponents' proofs
Shafi'i relies on a specific hadith about two qullas. Maliki relies on a general hadith about water being pure until changed. Understand each proof's apparent strength.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds: (1) The prohibition of urinating in standing water and the command to wash hands three times before dipping into a small vessel imply that small water becomes impure easily. (2) Malik's hadith is interpreted as referring to flowing water (to reconcile with other hadiths, specifically the incident of Bi'r Buda'ah). (3) The qullatayn hadith is weak (da'if) as stated by al-Bayhaqi and others, and even some Shafi'is (al-Ghazali, al-Rawiyani) abandoned it.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Hanafi law, a large pond (10×10) remains pure after a fall of impurity unless a change is detected. Under Shafi'i law, the same pond would be considered impure if it is less than two qullas, regardless of change. Under Maliki law, any quantity remains pure until a change occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نجس أي مطلقا سواء تغير أحد أوصافه أو لا، و"النجس" بفتح الجيم: عين النجاسة، وبكسرها: ما لا يكون طاهراً، والمراد هنا الأول. (مسكين) عشراً في عشر أي عشرة أذرع في عشرة أذرع، وقال الشافعي: يجوز إن كان قلتين، وهما خمس مائة رطل؛ لقوله عليه: "إذا بلغ الماء قلتين لم يحمل الخبث"، وقال مالك: يتوضأ به ما أحد أوصافه؛ لقوله عليه: "خلق الله الماء طهوراً لا ينجسه شيء إلا ما غير" الحديث، ولنا: نهيه عالية عن البول في الدائم، وعن غمس اليد في الإناء قبل أن يغسلها ثلاثاً، وما رواه الإمام مالك محمول على الماء الجاري توفيقاً بين الأحاديث؛ لأنه ورد في بئر بضاعة وماؤها كان جارياً في البساتين، وحديث القلتين ضعفه جماعة من المحدثين، حتى قال البيهقي من الشافعية: الحديث غير قوي، وقد تركه الغزالي والرؤياني مع شدة اتباعهما للشافعي لضعفه، فلا يعارض ما روينا. (فتح)
Najas means absolutely, whether one of its attributes changes or not. 'An-Najs' with fat-ha on jeem means the substance of impurity; with kasra means that which is not pure. The meaning here is the first. (Miskin) 'Ten by ten' means ten cubits by ten cubits. Al-Shafi'i said: It is permissible if it is two qullas, which are five hundred ratls, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'When water reaches two qullas, it does not carry impurity.' Malik said: Wudu is permissible as long as one of its attributes has not changed, due to his statement (peace be upon him): 'Allah created water as purifying; nothing makes it impure except what changes it' (the hadith). Our proof: His prohibition (peace be upon him) against urinating in standing water, and against dipping the hand in the vessel before washing it three times. The hadith narrated by Imam Malik is interpreted as referring to flowing water, to reconcile between the hadiths; because it was reported about the well of Bida'ah, and its water was flowing in gardens. The hadith of the two qullas has been declared weak by many hadith scholars, to the extent that al-Bayhaqi from the Shafi'is said: 'The hadith is not strong.' Al-Ghazali and al-Rawiyani left it, despite their strong adherence to Shafi'i, due to its weakness. So it cannot be used to contradict what we have narrated. (Fath)
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi school holds that a large pond of ten by ten cubits does not become impure by the mere fall of impurity; only a change in its attributes makes it impure. The text clarifies the meaning of 'najs' (with fat-ha = the substance of impurity; with kasra = impure entity). Then it presents the Shafi'i view: the criterion is quantity (two qullas, about 500 ratls) based on the hadith 'When water reaches two qullas, it does not carry impurity.' The Maliki view is that change in any attribute makes it impure, based on the hadith 'Water is pure except what changes it.' The Hanafi response: they rely on the prohibition of urinating in standing water and the command to wash hands three times, which imply that standing water is susceptible to impurity only if it is small. The hadith of Malik is interpreted as referring to flowing water (for reconciliation), and the qullatayn hadith is weak. This demonstrates Hanafi methodology: prioritizing stronger ahadith and reconciling conflicting ones.

Key Terms
النجس (al-najs (fat-ha) / al-najis (kasra)): With fat-ha: the substance of impurity (e.g., urine). With kasra: something not pure (ritually impure). Here the first meaning is intended.
القلتين (al-qullatayn): Two qullas, a specific quantity of water (about 500 ratls) used by Shafi'i as a threshold for impurity resistance.
الدائم (al-da'im): Standing water that does not flow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The impurity is visible
B) The water is less than ten by ten cubits
C) The water changes in color, taste, or smell
D) The impurity is from a dog

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school determines the impurity of standing water by size (10x10 cubits) and attribute change, prioritizing stronger hadiths over the weak qullatayn tradition.

Flowing Water and Animals Without Flowing Blood in Taharah
Defining flowing water and ruling on dead animals without flowing blood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of flowing water and apply the ruling that dead animals without flowing blood do not impurify water, distinguishing the Hanafi view from the Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Animals without flowing blood do not affect water purity; only their visible trace matters.

This lesson clarifies two key points of Hanafi taharah: what qualifies as flowing water, and the surprising ruling that dead insects, fish, and similar creatures do not make water impure. The proof from the hadith of Salman al-Farisi and a rational argument show the consistency of the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Define flowing water
Understand the Hanafi test for flowing water: water that moves with a piece of straw. Such water has a different ruling regarding impurity.
This is a practical custom, not a strict legal definition.
2
Identify the legal category
When impurity falls into flowing water, it remains pure unless a trace (color, taste, or odor) appears. If no trace, wudu is permissible.
3
Apply the rule to animals without flowing blood
The death of such animals (mosquitoes, flies, fish, etc.) in water does not render the water impure, even in small amounts. This is a specific Hanafi position.
Contrast with animals with flowing blood, whose death does impurify.
4
Examine the proof
Analyze the hadith of Salman al-Farisi where the Prophet said such water is halal for drinking and wudu. Also note the rational argument: these animals naturally live in water, so they lack flowing blood, and blood opposes water.
The dalil is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but supported by rational reasoning.
5
Note the disagreement
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that non-fish animals without flowing blood still impurify water. The Hanafi response relies on the hadith and the natural disposition of these animals.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is whether wudu is valid from such water.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالجاري، وهو ما يذهب بتبنةٍ فيتوضاً منه إن لم يُر أثره وهو طعم أو لون أو ريح. أي كالماء الجاري أي الماء الجاري أي الأثر وموت ما لا دم له فيه كالبق والذُّباب والزنبور والعقرب والسَّمَك والضفدع والسرطان الحيوان الماء وغيره من ا المائعات وكذا الحية بأنواعه لا ينجسه أثره: أي أثر النجس بعد وقوعه فيه، أما لو ظهر فيه أثره فإنه يكون نجساً. (ط) ريح: لأن هذه الأشياء مما يعلم وجودها حسا. (مستخلص) وموت إلخ : أي موت حيوان ليس له دم سائل في الماء الدائم القليل لا ينجسه خلافاً للشافعي في غير السمك له: أن التحريم لا بطريق الكراهة آية النجاسة، وتحليل أجزاء الميتة في الماء، بخلاف دودة الخل وسوس الثمار؛ لأن فيه ضرورة ولنا ما روى سلمان الفارسي ه أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سئل عن إناء فيه طعام أو شراب يموت فيه ما ليس له نفس سائلة، فقال له: "هذا هو الحلال أكله وشربه والوضوء منه". ثم الدليل على كون الدم معدوماً في هذه الحيوانات دوام سكونها في الماء؛ لأن الدموي لا يسكن في الماء؛ لمضادة بين الدم والماء طبعاً؛ لأن الماء بارد رطب، والدم حار رطب. (مستخلص ملخصاً) والضفدع بكسر الضاد وأراد به المائي دون البري. رمز الحقائق: ۲۰/۱] لا ينجسه: خبر المبتدأ، أي موت للشافعي
Like flowing water, which is water that moves with a piece of straw; one may perform wudu from it if no trace of impurity is seen – that trace being taste, color, or odor. The meaning: like flowing water, i.e., flowing water. The meaning of trace: and the death of what has no flowing blood, like mosquitoes, flies, wasps, scorpions, fish, frogs, and crabs, whether in water or other liquids, and likewise snakes of all kinds, does not make it impure. Its trace: i.e., the trace of impurity after it falls into it; if its trace appears, then it is impure. (T) Odor: because these things are known by sense. (Mukhtasar) And death etc.: i.e., the death of an animal without flowing blood in a small quantity of standing water does not make it impure, contrary to al-Shafi'i regarding non-fish. His evidence: prohibition by way of dislike is a sign of impurity, and the analysis of parts of the dead animal in water, except for vinegar worms and fruit weevils, because there is necessity. Our evidence is what Salman al-Farisi narrated: that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked about a vessel of food or drink in which something without a flowing soul dies, and he said: 'This is halal to eat, drink, and perform wudu from it.' And the evidence that blood is absent in these animals is their constant staying in water, because a blood-bearing animal does not stay in water due to the natural opposition between blood and water, as water is cold and moist while blood is hot and moist. (Summarized) And 'al-dafda'' (frog) is with kasra on the daal, and the intended is the aquatic one, not the land one. Rumor al-Haqaiq: 20/1. It does not make it impure: predicate of the subject, i.e., death for al-Shafi'i.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct rulings. First, flowing water is defined as water that moves with a straw (i.e., it flows). Such water is permissible for wudu if no trace of impurity (color, taste, odor) is seen after impurity falls. Second, animals without flowing blood (e.g., flies, fish, frogs) do not render water impure if they die in it, according to the Hanafi school, contrary to Shafi'i. The proof is the hadith of Salman al-Farisi, and a rational argument: animals that stay in water naturally lack flowing blood because blood and water are naturally opposed. The Hanafi position is based on this strong evidence, and the Shafi'i view is rejected.

Key Terms
الجاري (al-jari): Flowing water, defined as water that moves with a piece of straw.
تبنة (tibna): A piece of straw; used to test if water is flowing.
لا دم له سائل (la dam lahu sa'il): An animal that has no flowing blood; includes insects, fish, reptiles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The water becomes impure and cannot be used
B) The water remains pure and can be used for wudu
C) The water becomes makruh but still valid
D) It depends on the size of the water container

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi taharah, animals without flowing blood do not affect water purity; the water remains permissible for wudu unless a trace of impurity appears.

Used Water (Al-Ma' al-Musta'mal) – Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on used water with tarjih

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three dimensions of the intra-Hanafi dispute on used water and identify the relied-upon view (mufta bihi).

Core Idea
Used water is pure (tahir) but not purifying (mutahhir) – the relied-upon Hanafi view.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents the internal Hanafi debate on water that has been used for wudu or worship. The author, al-Marghinani, surveys the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and others, then chooses Muhammad's position as the most sound and operative for fatwa. Understanding this dispute shows how the Hanafi school weights different transmissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three contested issues
The passage frames the dispute around three questions: (1) What act causes water to become 'used'? (2) At what moment does it become used? (3) What is its legal ruling (hukm)?
These are the three 'intha' al-khilaf' points.
2
List the positions for each issue
For each question, note the differences among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and sometimes Zufar. For example, on the first issue: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say raf' al-hadath or qurba; Muhammad says only qurba.
Pay attention to the labels: 'inda'l-Hanafi, 'indahu, etc.
3
Identify the author's chosen view
Al-Marghinani states 'wa ikhtara al-musannif hadha' for the third issue, and explicitly says Muhammad's view is 'zahir al-riwaya' and 'alayhi al-fatwa'.
This is the mufta bihi position.
4
Note the authority weight
The mufta bihi is supported by being the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), which is the strongest authority level in the Hanafi school.
The other views are not rejected but are considered marjuh (weaker).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والماء المستعمل لقربة أو رفع حدثٍ إذا استقرّ في مكان طاهر لا مطهر، أي الماء المستعمل للأحداث كالجاري أي عندنا وعمدة الشافعي في هذا الباب على القلّتين، فإذا بلغهما لا يتنجس إلا بالتغير، وبه قال أحمد، وعند مالك على التغير مطلقاً. [رمز الحقائق: ۱۹/۱] بتبنة: وقيل: ما يعده الناس جارياً وهو الأصح. [رمز الحقائق: ١٩/١] منه: أي من الماء الجاري أو الماء الدائم. (ط) إن لم ير أثره أي أثر النجاسة بعد وقوعها فيه، ثم إذا لم يتنجس كله هل يتنجس موضع الوقوع؟ فإن كانت مرئية يتنجس ،وإلا فلا وعند عامة مشايخ العراق يتنجس موضع الوقوع فيهما، وفي "النصاب": الفتوى على أنه لا يتنجس موضع الوقوع مطلقا إذا كان عشراً في عشر إلا بالتغير فيهما، أي المرئية وغيرها. (مسكين وغيره) أثره: أي أثر النجس بعد وقوعه فيه، أما لو ظهر فيه أثره فإنه يكون نجساً. (ط) ريح: لأن هذه الأشياء مما يعلم وجودها حسا. (مستخلص) وموت إلخ : أي موت حيوان ليس له دم سائل في الماء الدائم القليل لا ينجسه خلافاً للشافعي في غير السمك له: أن التحريم لا بطريق الكراهة آية النجاسة، وتحليل أجزاء الميتة في الماء، بخلاف دودة الخل وسوس الثمار؛ لأن فيه ضرورة ولنا ما روى سلمان الفارسي ه أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم سئل عن إناء فيه طعام أو شراب يموت فيه ما ليس له نفس سائلة، فقال له: "هذا هو الحلال أكله وشربه والوضوء منه". ثم الدليل على كون الدم معدوماً في هذه الحيوانات دوام سكونها في الماء؛ لأن الدموي لا يسكن في الماء؛ لمضادة بين الدم والماء طبعاً؛ لأن الماء بارد رطب، والدم حار رطب. (مستخلص ملخصاً) والضفدع بكسر الضاد وأراد به المائي دون البري. رمز الحقائق: ۲۰/۱] لا ينجسه: خبر المبتدأ، أي موت للشافعي والماء المستعمل إلخ : اعلم أن في الماء المستعمل اختلافات كثيرة، مستعملاً؟ فعند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف برفع الحدث أو بالاستعمال على وجه القربة، وقال محمد: أنه يصير مستعملاً باستعماله على وجه القربة. والثاني: متى يصير مستعملاً؟ فعند أبي حنيفة: كلما زال عن العضو صار مستعملاً، وقالا : إذا استقر في مكان واحد، واختار المصنف هذا لمكان الضرورة. والثالث: في حكمه، فقال مالك وهو أحد قولي الشافعي إنه مطهر، وقال زفر إن كان مستعمله متوضياً كان الماء طهوراً، وإن كان محدثاً فطاهر غير مطهر ، وقال أبو حنيفة وأبو يوسف هو نجس، فعند أبي حنيفة بالنجاسة المغلظة، وعند أبي يوسف بالمخففة، وقال محمد: هو طاهر غير مطهر واختار المصنف هذا، وهو ظاهر الرواية، وعليه الفتوى مستخلص) وغيره لقربة : أي لأجل تقرب إلى الله بأن توضأ على وضوء. في مكان سواء كان أرضاً أو إناء أو كف المتوضئ، وقيل: يصير مستعملا بمجرد الانفصال من العضو وإن لم يستقر في مكان، قيل: هو الصحيح رمز الحقائق ٢٠/١] طاهر : بالرفع على أنه خبر الماء.
Used water, whether for a supererogatory act (qurba) or for removing an impurity (hadath), when it settles in a pure place, it is not purifying (mutahhir). Meaning: used water for events. Like flowing water, according to us. The basis for al-Shafi'i in this chapter is the two qullas; if it reaches them, it does not become impure except by change; Ahmad agreed. According to Malik, it is change absolutely. [Rumor al-Haqaiq: 19/1] With a straw: and it is said that what people consider as flowing is the correct view. [Rumor al-Haqaiq: 19/1] From it: i.e., from flowing water or standing water. (T) If its trace is not seen, i.e., the trace of impurity after it falls into it, then if the entire water does not become impure, does the place of impact become impure? If it is visible, it becomes impure; otherwise, no. According to the general body of Iraqi scholars, the place of impact becomes impure in both cases. In al-Nisab: the fatwa is that the place of impact does not become impure at all if it is ten by ten, except by change in both, i.e., visible and other. (Miskin and others) Its trace: i.e., the trace of the impurity after it falls into it; but if its trace appears, then it is impure. (T) Odor: because these things are known by sense. (Mukhtasar) And death etc.: i.e., the death of an animal without flowing blood in a small quantity of standing water does not make it impure, contrary to al-Shafi'i regarding non-fish. His evidence: prohibition by way of dislike is a sign of impurity, and the analysis of parts of the dead animal in water, except for vinegar worms and fruit weevils, because there is necessity. Our evidence is what Salman al-Farisi narrated: that the Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked about a vessel of food or drink in which something without a flowing soul dies, and he said: 'This is halal to eat, drink, and perform wudu from it.' And the evidence that blood is absent in these animals is their constant staying in water, because a blood-bearing animal does not stay in water due to the natural opposition between blood and water, as water is cold and moist while blood is hot and moist. (Summarized) And 'al-dafda'' (frog) is with kasra on the daal, and the intended is the aquatic one, not the land one. Rumor al-Haqaiq: 20/1. It does not make it impure: predicate of the subject, i.e., death for al-Shafi'i. And used water etc.: Know that there are many differences in used water, (first) what makes it used? According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: by removing hadath or by using for the sake of qurba. Muhammad said: It becomes used by using for the sake of qurba. Second: When does it become used? According to Abu Hanifa: as soon as it leaves the limb, it becomes used. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: when it settles in one place. The author chose this (the latter) due to necessity. Third: Its ruling. Malik and one view of al-Shafi'i said it is purifying (mutahhir). Zufar said: If the user was in a state of wudu, the water is tahur (purifying); if hadath, then tahir (pure) not mutahhir. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf said it is najis (impure); according to Abu Hanifa it is severely impure, according to Abu Yusuf lightly impure. Muhammad said: it is tahir (pure) not mutahhir. The author chose this, and it is the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya), and on it is the fatwa. (Mukhtasar) And 'ghayri qurba': i.e., for the sake of drawing near to Allah by performing wudu after wudu. In a place, whether earth, vessel, or the palm of the one performing wudu. And it is said: it becomes used simply by separation from the limb even if it does not settle in a place. It is said that this is correct. (Rumor al-Haqaiq 20/1) 'Tahir' is raised (nominative) as the predicate of 'water'.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the ruling of used water (ma' musta'mal). The author first states the basic ruling: used water that settles in a clean place is not purifying (mutahhir). Then he presents the differences in three issues: (1) What makes water used? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say removing hadath or any act of worship; Muhammad says only act of worship. (2) When does it become used? Abu Hanifa says immediately upon leaving the limb; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say only after settling. (3) What is its hukm? Malik and one Shafi'i view say it is mutahhir; Zufar distinguishes based on the user's state; Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say it is najis; Muhammad says it is tahir (pure) but not mutahhir. The author (Marghinani) chooses Muhammad's view, which is zahir al-riwaya and the basis for fatwa. This shows the internal diversity within the Hanafi school and the process of preferring one view over others.

Key Terms
الماء المستعمل (al-ma' al-musta'mal): Used water, i.e., water that has been used for wudu or similar acts.
رفع الحدث (raf' al-hadath): Removing ritual impurity (i.e., performing wudu).
القربة (al-qurba): A supererogatory act of worship to draw near to Allah.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The apparent narration, i.e., the well-established transmission in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view (mufta bihi) stated in this passage, what is the ruling of water that has been used for wudu?
A) It is najis (impure)
B) It is mutahhir (purifying)
C) It is tahir (pure) but not mutahhir
D) It is makruh

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon position (Muhammad's) is that used water is tahir (pure) but not mutahhir (purifying), and this is the basis for fatwa.

The Well Issue and the Mnemonic J-H-T
Intra-Hanafi dispute on well contamination by a junub diver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on the purity of water and a junub diver in a well and explain the conditions that differentiate them.

Core Idea
The letters J-H-T encode three Hanafi rulings: only Abu Hanifa rules both impure; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule both pure on different grounds.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. A junub person dives into a well to retrieve a bucket. The purity of the water and the diver's body depend on which condition each imam considers necessary for water to become musta'mal (used). The commentator elaborates on the scenario and later adds restrictions to avoid unanimous corruption. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to trace how usul principles generate different fiqh rulings within the same school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the three positions
Read the passage carefully. The scenario is a man who is junub diving into a well to retrieve a bucket. Note the three rulings: Abu Hanifa says both water and man become impure (najis); Abu Yusuf says both remain as they were (pure); Muhammad says both remain pure.
The acronym J-H-T stands for the initial letters of the ruling: J for najis, H for hal (state), T for tahir.
2
Examine the condition for musta'mal water for each imam
Abu Hanifa holds that upon first contact with the junub body, the water becomes musta'mal and thus impure. Abu Yusuf conditions musta'mal water on the act of pouring (sabb), which is absent when diving. Muhammad conditions musta'mal water on the intent (niyyah) to remove hadath, which is absent when diving for the bucket.
These conditions are derived from the definitions of musta'mal water in Hanafi usul.
3
Understand the mnemonic order
The letters J-H-T correspond to the chronological order of the imams: J for Abu Hanifa (first), H for Abu Yusuf (second), T for Muhammad (third). This order is not arbitrary but reflects their seniority in the school.
The commentator explicitly states this order: 'the Imam is foremost, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad'.
4
Apply the later restrictions from al-Fath
Al-Fath adds that the diving must be for retrieving a bucket or cooling, not for intended ghusl (bathing). If intended ghusl, according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (but not Abu Yusuf's own condition of sabb), the water becomes musta'mal. Also, the diver must have performed istinja with water, not stones; otherwise the water becomes corrupt by consensus.
These restrictions prevent the water from becoming musta'mal unanimously or create consensus on impurity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة ٢٦ مياه الوضوء ف ك ومسألة البئر جحط. ومسألة البئر إلخ ومسألة البئر كلام إضافي مبتدأ، وقوله: "جحط" في موضع الرفع على الخبرية، تقديره: مسألة البئر [الخلافية] يضبط فيها بحروف "جحط"، فالجيم من النجس، والحاء من الحال والطاء من الطاهر، وصورة المسألة: رجل انغمس في البئر لطلب الدلو وهو جنب، فالماء والرجل نجسان عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن بأول الملاقاة تنجس الماء والرجل على حاله لنجاسة الماء المستعمل عنده وعنه: الرجل طاهر في الأصح، وعند أبي يوسف: كلاهما بحاله، أما الرجل فلعدم الصب الذي هو شرط عنده، وأما الماء فلعدم التقرب أو إزالة الحدث، وعند محمد: كلاهما طاهران، أما الرجل فلعدم اشتراط الصب، وأما الماء فلعدم التقرب، وإنما دلت هذه الحروف الثلاثة على أصحابنا الثلاثة بهذا الترتيب؛ لأنهم على هذا الترتيب في الخارج، فإن الإمام هو المقدم، ثم أبو يوسف، ثم محمد له؛ فلذلك قدم الحرف الذي دل على قول أبي حنيفة، ثم الحرف الذي دل على قول أبي يوسف، ثم الحرف الذي دل على قول محمد. [رمز الحقائق: ۲۰/۱] وقال في "الفتح": قيد الانغماس للدلو ومثله التبرد؛ للاحتراز عما لو أراد الاغتسال حيث يصير مستعملا عندهما، خلافاً لأبي يوسف لاشتراطه الصب في غير الماء الجاري، وقيده منه، ملا مسكين بكون الرجل مستنجياً بالماء؛ للاحتراز عما لو استنجى بالأحجار حيث يفسد الماء بالاتفاق.
Book of Purification, twenty-six, the waters of wudu. And the issue of the well (abbreviated as J-H-T). Explanation: 'And the issue of the well' is additional speech, a subject. His saying 'J-H-T' is in the nominative case as predicate. Its interpretation: the [disputed] well issue is summarized by the letters J-H-T. The J is for impure, the H for state, and the T for pure. The scenario: A man dove into the well to retrieve a bucket while he was in a state of major impurity (junub). So according to Abu Hanifa, both the water and the man are impure, because at the first contact the water became impure and the man remains in his state due to the impurity of used water in his view. And from him (another narration): the man is pure in the most correct view. According to Abu Yusuf: both remain as they were. As for the man, because there is no pouring (sabb) which is a condition in his view, and as for the water, because there is no nearness or removal of hadath. According to Muhammad: both are pure. As for the man, because pouring is not a condition, and as for the water, because there is no nearness. These three letters indicate our three imams in this order because they are in this order in existence: the Imam is foremost, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad. So the letter indicating Abu Hanifa's statement is placed first, then the letter indicating Abu Yusuf's statement, then the letter indicating Muhammad's statement. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 1/20). And it is said in al-Fath: He restricted the diving to retrieving the bucket, and similarly for cooling, to avoid the case where he intended to bathe, for then it becomes used water according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Abu Yusuf because he conditions pouring in other than running water. And he restricted it with 'from it' (?), and Mulla Miskin added that the man should be one who performed istinja with water, to avoid the case where he performed istinja with stones, for then the water becomes corrupt by consensus.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the status of water and a junub person when the person dives into a well to retrieve a bucket. The matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq presents the acronym 'J-H-T' as a mnemonic for the three positions of the founding Hanafi imams. The commentator (probably Imam al-Zayla'i or others) elaborates on the scenario. Abu Hanifa holds that both water and man become impure because the water becomes musta'mal (used) immediately upon contact, and this used water was considered impure in his view. However, there is a narration from him that the man remains pure. Abu Yusuf maintains that both remain as they were: the man is pure because there was no intentional pouring (sabb) which he requires for water to become musta'mal, and the water remains pure because there was no nearness (taqarrub) or intent to remove hadath. Muhammad holds that both are pure because he does not require pouring for water to become musta'mal, and the water remains pure due to lack of intent. The commentary then explains the logic behind ordering the letters J, H, T to correspond to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad respectively, reflecting their chronological order in the school. Later, al-Fath adds conditions: the diving must be for retrieving a bucket or cooling, not for intended ghusl, and the person must have used water for istinja, not stones, to avoid unanimous corruption. This passage is crucial for understanding the Hanafi ikhtilaf on water purity and the conditions for water becoming musta'mal.

Key Terms
جحط (j-h-t): An acronym where J stands for najis (impure), H for hal (state), and T for tahir (pure), summarizing the three positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad respectively on the well issue.
انغمس (inghamasa): To dive or plunge into water, specifically in the context of entering a well.
مستعمل (musta'mal): Water that has been used for wudu or ghusl and is no longer considered pure for further acts of purification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad
D) All three agree

Key Takeaway
The mnemonic J-H-T summarizes three valid Hanafi positions on the well issue; the ruling depends on each imam's condition for water becoming musta'mal, and the majority view (both pure) is often preferred.

Tanning of Hides: Hanafi Ruling and Inter-Madhhab Disputes
Ruling on purification of hides by tanning, with exceptions and inter-madhhab disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on purification of hides by tanning, identify the exceptional cases, and evaluate the Shafi'i and Maliki objections.

Core Idea
Tanning purifies every hide except pig and human; dog leather is permissible per the Hanafi fatwa.

The purification of animal hides through tanning is a practical issue for daily prayer and use. This lesson walks through the Hanafi position, its proofs, and how it differs from the Shafi'i and Maliki schools, clarifying what leathers are permissible for worship.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on tanning
Identify the core ruling: tanning purifies every hide except pig and human skin. Note that tanning can be done by real agents or legal means like sun-drying.
The ruling is based on the hadith 'Any hide that is tanned has become purified.'
2
Identify the exceptions and their reasons
Pig skin is excluded due to its essential impurity (Quran 6:145). Human skin is technically purified but prohibited from use because of human dignity.
Do not confuse technical purity with permissibility of use.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i requires a specific tree-bark treatment (tashmith) and holds that only hides from edible animals are purified.
This restricts the generality of the hadith.
4
Analyze the Maliki position
Imam Malik holds that no dead animal hide is purified, citing a prohibition from the Prophet.
The Hanafi response distinguishes between tanned and untanned hides.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response and thamarah
The Hanafi school responds that the prohibition refers to untanned hides, and the general hadith overrides. The practical difference: Hanafis allow use of tanned dog leather, while others do not.
The mufta bihi position is that dog is not najis al-ayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل إهاب دبغ فقد طهر إلا جلد الخنزير والآدمي، ظاهرا وباطنا ولا يعود نجسا بإصابته ماء. وكل إهاب دبغ الدباغ ما يمنع النتن والفساد ولو تشميساً أو تتريباً، وعند الشافعي: يشترط التشئيث، أي الإصلاح بشجر، مثل التفاح يدبغ بورقه والمراد أن الإهاب إذا دبغ فقد جازت الصلاة فيه والوضوء والشرب وقال مالك: لا يطهر جلد الميتة؛ لنهي النبي عنه عن الانتفاع بالميتة بإهاب ولنا عموم قوله عليه: "أي إهاب دبغ فقد طهر"، ولا حجة له فيما يرويه؛ لأن الإهاب اسم لغير المدبوغ، فلا شك في عدم جواز الانتفاع منه قبل الدبغ، قاله في "المستخلص"، وقال في "فتح المعين": مقتضى "كل إهاب إلخ" طهارة جلد الكلب بالدباغ؛ بناء على ما هو المفتى به من أنه ليس بنجس العين، وتشمل لكل ما يؤكل وما لا يؤكل، وفي الفيل خلاف محمد، والمراد بالإهاب ما هو القابل للدباغ، وما لا يقبله لا يطهر به كجلد الحية الصغيرة والفارة، كذا لا يطهر بالذكاة؛ لأن الذكاة إنما تقام مقام الدباغ فيما يحتمله أما قميص الحية فطاهر. اعلم أنه لا يدخل في قول من قال بنجاسة عين الكلب الشعر بخلاف قولهم بنجاسة عين الخنزير فإنه يدخل فيه شعره أيضاً. وعند الشافعي: كل ما لا يؤكل لحمه لا يطهر جلده بالدباغة، كذا في النهاية"، والنهي في ما رواه مالك عن الانتفاع محمول على ما كان من جهة الأكل. دبغ: بدباغة حقيقية كالقرظ والشب ونحوهما، أو حكمية كالتتريب والتشميس والإلقاء في الريح. [رمز الحقائق: ٢١/١] الخنزير والآدمي: قدم الخنزير؛ لأن الموضع موضع إهانة، والمراد بالاستثناء من "طهر" عدم جواز الاستعمال؛ لتصريحهم بأن جلد الآدمي يطهر بالدباغة، لكن لا يجوز استعماله لكرامته، فجهة عدم الجواز في جلديهما مختلفة، ولا يطهر جلد الخنزير بالدبغ لنجاسة عينه؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَإِنَّهُ رِجْسٌ (الأنعام: ١٤٥)؛ إذ الضمير راجع إلى الخنزير؛
And every hide that is tanned has become purified, except the skin of the pig and the human, outwardly and inwardly, and it does not become impure by being hit by water. And every hide: tanning is what prevents stench and decay, even if by sun-drying or by earth. According to al-Shafi'i, he stipulates 'tashmith', meaning treatment by tree bark, such as apple tree leaves for tanning. The meaning is that when a hide is tanned, prayer in it, wudu, and drinking are permissible. Malik said: The skin of a dead animal is not purified because of the prohibition of the Prophet (peace be upon him) from benefiting from a dead animal's hide. And we have the generality of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Any hide that is tanned has become purified.' And there is no proof for him in what he narrates, because 'ihab' is a name for untanned skin, so there is no doubt that benefiting from it before tanning is impermissible. It is said in 'al-Mustakhlas' and 'Fath al-Mu'in': The implication of 'every hide etc.' is the purification of dog's skin by tanning, based on the fatwa that it is not intrinsically impure (najis al-ayn). And it includes everything that is eaten and not eaten. Regarding the elephant, there is a difference from Muhammad. The meaning of 'ihab' is that which is susceptible to tanning; what is not susceptible is not purified by it, like the skin of a small snake and a mouse. Similarly, it is not purified by slaughter because slaughter only stands in place of tanning in what can bear it. As for the snake's slough, it is pure. Know that it does not enter into the statement of those who say dog is impure by essence, the hair, unlike their statement of the pig's essential impurity, for that includes its hair as well. According to al-Shafi'i: every animal whose meat is not eaten, its skin is not purified by tanning. So in 'al-Nihaya'. And the prohibition in what Malik narrated about benefiting is taken to mean benefiting by eating. Tanning: by real tanning like qaraz and alum and the like, or legal tanning like dusting, sun-drying, or throwing in the wind. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 21/1]. Regarding the pig and human: he mentioned the pig first because the context is one of humiliation. The meaning of the exception from 'purified' is the impermissibility of use, because they explicitly stated that human skin is purified by tanning, but it is impermissible to use it due to its honor. So the reason for impermissibility in the two skins is different. And the pig's skin is not purified by tanning due to its essential impurity, because of His saying: 'Indeed, it is filth' (6:145); the pronoun refers to the pig.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with inter-madhhab dispute concerning the purification of hides through tanning. The matn states that tanning purifies every hide except pig and human skin, and that after tanning, the hide does not revert to impurity by contact with water. The commentary defines tanning as any process that prevents decay, including sun-drying and earth treatment. The Shafi'i school requires a specific tree-bark treatment (tashmith). The Hanafi respond to Malik's objection by citing the hadith 'Any hide that is tanned has become purified' and argue that the Prophet's prohibition refers to untanned hides. The Hanafi fatwa extends purification to dog's skin since the dog is not najis al-ayn (intrinsically impure) in the mufta bihi view. The passage also distinguishes pig and human: pig is excluded due to its essential impurity as per Quran 6:145, while human skin is technically purified but cannot be used due to human dignity. This shows the Hanafi position that tanning removes impurity except for substances considered intrinsically impure (najis al-ayn). The commentary further discusses that shaving off snake skin is pure without tanning.

Key Terms
إهاب (ihab): The skin of an animal, specifically untanned hide; after tanning it is called 'adim' or 'jild'.
دباغ (dibagh): Tanning: process that prevents stench and decay, whether by actual tanning agents or by legal means like sun-drying.
نجس العين (najis al-ayn): Intrinsically impure substance that cannot be purified by any means.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Pig skin
B) Human skin
C) Dog skin
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
Tanning purifies every hide except pig (due to essential impurity) and human (due to dignity); the Hanafi school permits use of tanned dog leather in worship.

Purity of Hair and Bones from Humans and Dead Animals
Ruling on hair and bones of humans and dead animals being pure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the purity of hair and bones from humans and dead animals and explain the inter-madhhab dispute with the Shafi'i and Maliki positions.

Core Idea
Hair and bones of dead animals are pure in the Hanafi school—only soft, liquid parts become impure.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq establishes a distinctive Hanafi ruling: the hair and bones of both humans and dead animals are pure (tahir). While the Shafi'i school considers all parts of a dead animal impure, and the Maliki school makes a distinction between hair and bones, the Hanafi school holds that dry, solid parts like hair and bones are not ritually impure. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the Hanafi principle that ritual impurity (najasa) attaches primarily to liquids and moist substances, not to dry solids.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of the ruling
The passage discusses two items: hair (sha'r) and bones ('azm), from two sources: humans and dead animals. Note the grammatical structure: 'and their bones' is nominative, conjoined to 'hair'.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
Hair and bones are pure (tahiran). The commentator clarifies the grammar and then introduces the ikhtilaf.
3
Locate the opposing schools and their positions
Al-Shafi'i considers both impure (najisan). Malik considers hair pure but bones impure.
4
Identify the Hanafi proof principle
The Hanafi proof is based on the principle that only what is intrinsically najis or specified becomes impure. Hair and bones are not affected by death because they lack the moisture that carries impurity.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Hanafis allow use and sale of hair and bones from dead animals; Shafi'is and Malikis (with their variation) do not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشعر الإنسان والميتة وعظمُهما طاهران. شعر الميتة غير المنتوف. كلام إضافي، "والميتة" عطف على الإنسان، و"عظمها" بالرفع عطف على الشعر، أي عظم الإنسان والميتة، وقوله : "طاهران" خبر المبتدأ والمعطوف عليه، وعند الشافعي هما نجسان، وقال مالك: شعر الميتة طاهر دون عظمها رمز الحقائق: ٢١/١]
And hair of a human and a dead animal, and their bones, are pure. Hair of a dead animal that is not plucked. Additional speech: 'and the dead animal' is an appositive to 'human', and 'their bones' is in the nominative as an appositive to 'hair', meaning bones of the human and the dead animal. His saying 'both are pure' is the predicate of the subject and what is conjoined to it. According to al-Shafi'i, both are impure. Malik said: hair of a dead animal is pure without its bone. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 21/1]
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement establishing the purity of human and dead animal hair and bones in the Hanafi school. The matn asserts that hair of a human and a dead animal, as well as their bones, are tahir (pure). The commentator clarifies the grammatical structure and then notes the inter-madhhab ikhtilaf: al-Shafi'i considers both impure, while Malik makes a distinction between hair (pure) and bones (impure). The Hanafi position is based on the principle that what is not considered ritually impure from a living human or animal remains pure after death except for the substances specifically excluded (like blood, meat). Hair and bones are not considered najis because they are not affected by the same decay that makes flesh impure. The phrase 'shahr al-maytati ghayr al-mantuf' (hair of a dead animal that is not plucked) indicates that the ruling applies to hair that is naturally attached, not plucked from a living animal. This is a clear example of Hanafi differentiation from other madhahib on the purity of hard, dry parts of the dead animal.

Key Terms
شعر (sha'r): Hair from humans or animals, considered pure in Hanafi fiqh even after death if not plucked from a dead animal.
عظم (azm): Bone, considered pure even from a dead animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Impure
B) Pure
C) Pure only if not plucked
D) Impure only if from a pig

Key Takeaway
Hanafis rule hair and bones of dead animals as pure, distinguishing them from flesh and blood, based on the principle that impurity does not transfer to solid, dry parts.
